• Practical tools to challenge negative self-beliefs and cultivate self-compassion
• Effective techniques to set healthy boundaries and reclaim your personal power
• Strategies to reconnect with your authentic self and find purpose in your life
• Ways to manage anxiety, triggers, and emotional overwhelm during the recovery process
• Guidance on building a supportive network and accessing resources for ongoing healing

💛 Support the show: https://www.spreaker.com/podcast/c-ptsd-let-s-make-sense-of-this-sh-t--6331440/support"	"Hey there, fellow survivors and warriors on this healing journey. I'm Gregory, and you're tuning in to ""CPTSD: Let's Make Sense of This Shit."" Today, we're diving deep into something that hits home for so many of us – rebuilding self-worth after complex trauma. And let me tell you, if you're struggling with feeling ""not enough"" or wondering if you'll ever feel whole again, you're not alone in this. Not even close.

You know, it's funny – well, not funny-haha, but funny-interesting – how CPTSD has this sneaky way of dismantling our sense of self-worth piece by piece, often so gradually that we don't even notice it happening. One day we wake up and wonder where that confident, joyful person we used to be – or could have been – disappeared to. Trust me, I've been there, and it's like trying to build a house on quicksand.

In today's episode, we're going to unpack this mess together. We'll start by getting real about what CPTSD does to our self-worth – the science behind it, the emotional toll, and why traditional self-help advice often misses the mark for trauma survivors. I've got some powerful listener stories to share that I think will resonate with many of you – stories that remind us we're all in this together, fighting the same invisible battles.

But here's the thing – we're not just here to commiserate. I'm going to walk you through some practical, trauma-informed techniques that actually work for rebuilding self-worth from the ground up. None of that ""just think positive"" bullshit – we're talking real, evidence-based strategies that acknowledge the complexity of trauma recovery.

And because I know how overwhelming this work can be, I'll be sharing some resources and next steps that you can explore at your own pace. Remember, healing isn't a race, and you don't have to figure it all out today. Sometimes just showing up and listening is the most powerful thing you can do for yourself.

This episode is especially close to my heart because rebuilding self-worth is such a fundamental part of healing from CPTSD. It's like the foundation we need to build before anything else can truly stick. And while it might feel impossible right now, I promise you – with the right tools and support, it is absolutely possible to rediscover your worth and build it stronger than ever.

So grab your favorite comfort beverage, find a cozy spot, and let's dig into this together. Whether you're just starting to understand your CPTSD or you're well along in your healing journey, there's something here for you. And remember, as we always say here – you're not broken, you're healing. And healing, messy as it is, is always possible.

Let's make sense of this shit together, shall we?

So let me share something personal with you about self-worth, because honestly, this topic hits really close to home. You know those moments when you're standing in front of the mirror, and that inner critic starts running its mouth? Yeah, I've been there – probably spent way too many years there, if I'm being real with you.

I remember this one time, about six years ago, when I was supposed to give this important presentation at work. And there I was, sitting in my car outside the building, completely frozen. Not because I wasn't prepared – I'd practiced that presentation until I could recite it in my sleep. No, I was frozen because that voice in my head kept insisting I wasn't ""enough."" Not smart enough, not qualified enough, not worthy enough to be in that room.

Now, here's where it gets interesting. Back then, I didn't have the language for what I was experiencing. I just thought I was fundamentally flawed, you know? Like somehow everyone else got this manual for life that I'd missed out on. But what I've come to understand – and what I want you to really hear – is that this wasn't some personal defect. This was CPTSD working its sneaky little magic on my sense of self-worth.

The thing about self-worth and CPTSD is that they're like oil and water – they don't naturally mix. When you've grown up in an environment where your worth was constantly questioned, dismissed, or straight-up denied, your brain actually rewires itself to maintain that narrative. It's like your nervous system gets stuck in this loop of ""not enough-ness.""

But here's the plot twist – and I love a good plot twist – that narrative isn't actually yours. It's a hand-me-down story that you've been carrying around, probably for years. Think about it: if you had a plant, and every day someone came by and told that plant it was worthless, would that make it any less of a plant? No, but it might affect how that plant grows.

The same goes for us. The messages we received during our formative years – whether through obvious trauma or those subtle, death-by-a-thousand-cuts kind of experiences – they shaped our self-worth, but they don't define it. And that's what we're going to dig into today.

You know what's wild? When I first started this healing journey, I thought rebuilding self-worth was going to be about positive affirmations and maybe some fancy meditation techniques. Don't get me wrong, those things can be helpful tools, but the real work? It's so much more nuanced than that. It's about excavating those old beliefs, examining them under the light of adult understanding, and slowly – sometimes painfully slowly – building new neural pathways.

I had this breakthrough moment in therapy (shoutout to my therapist Sarah, who probably isn't listening but deserves a medal anyway). She asked me to imagine my younger self, that kid who was trying so hard to be perfect, to be worthy of love and attention. And instead of feeling the usual shame and frustration, for the first time, I felt this overwhelming compassion. Like, damn, that kid was doing the best he could with what he had.

That's when it hit me – rebuilding self-worth isn't about becoming worthy. You already are worthy. It's about unlearning all the stuff that made you believe you weren't. And let me tell you, that's both the hardest and most liberating realization I've ever had.

Throughout this episode, we're going to explore this journey together. We'll look at how CPTSD specifically impacts self-worth, share some stories from our amazing community (with their permission, of course), and dig into practical tools that actually work in the real world – not just in theory.

What makes this conversation so important is that self-worth isn't just some fluffy self-help concept. It's the foundation of how we move through the world, how we set boundaries, how we choose relationships, and how we handle life's inevitable challenges. When you're operating from a place of compromised self-worth, it's like trying to build a house on quicksand. Everything feels unstable because, well, it is.

But here's the good news – and I mean really good news: just like our brains adapted to protect us during trauma, they can adapt again to support our healing. The science behind this is fascinating, and we'll get into that too. Because understanding how your brain works can be incredibly empowering when you're doing this deep work of rebuilding self-worth.

So grab your metaphorical hard hat, because we're about to do some serious reconstruction work. And remember, you're not alone in this. Every single person listening right now who's dealing with CPTSD has their own version of this struggle. We're all in this together, figuring it out one day at a time.

So let's really dig into what happens when CPTSD takes a wrecking ball to our sense of self-worth, because this is where the rubber meets the road. You know that feeling when you look in the mirror and the person staring back feels... wrong somehow? Like you're wearing someone else's skin? Yeah, that's what we're talking about.

Here's what I've learned from my own journey and from working with countless survivors: when trauma repeatedly tells us we're not enough, we start believing it's our default setting. It's like someone installed faulty software in our brains, and now every experience gets filtered through this ""I'm worthless"" lens. But here's the kicker – that software wasn't part of your original operating system. You weren't born thinking you were worthless.

Let me share something that happened to me last week. I was giving a presentation, and I made a small mistake – literally just stumbled over a word. No big deal, right? But my inner critic, that voice that CPTSD loves to amplify, started screaming: ""You're a fraud. Everyone knows you're a mess. Why do you even try?"" Sound familiar? That's the legacy of complex trauma talking, not reality.

The thing is, rebuilding self-worth isn't about positive affirmations or trying to convince yourself you're awesome. Trust me, I've tried that route, and it's about as effective as putting a Band-Aid on a broken leg. What actually works is understanding how your worth got damaged in the first place. Think of it like being an archeologist of your own history – you've got to carefully uncover the layers to see what's really going on underneath.

For many of us with CPTSD, our sense of worth was compromised during those critical developmental years when we were supposed to be learning that we mattered. Maybe you had a parent who was physically present but emotionally checked out. Or perhaps you were the family scapegoat, always somehow wrong, always somehow to blame. These experiences don't just disappear when we grow up – they become the foundation we build our adult lives on.

Now, here's where it gets interesting – and by interesting, I mean totally infuriating. Our brains, trying to protect us, actually start to resist building self-worth. It's like there's this internal saboteur saying, ""Hold up! Low self-worth is familiar. Low self-worth is safe. If we start feeling good about ourselves, something bad might happen!"" I call this the ""worth wobble"" – that uncomfortable feeling when you start to succeed or feel good about yourself, and suddenly your brain hits the panic button.

But here's the truth bomb I want to drop right now: your worth isn't actually damaged. It never was. What's damaged is your ability to access and believe in your inherent worth. Think about that for a second. Your worth is like the sun – it's always there, even when clouds of trauma block it from view.

The reconstruction process starts with understanding this fundamental truth. It's not about building something new; it's about clearing away the debris that's keeping you from seeing what was always there. And yes, this process can feel overwhelming. There will be days when you want to throw in the towel and go back to the familiar comfort of self-doubt. That's normal. That's human.

Let me tell you about a client – we'll call her Sarah – who perfectly illustrated this process. Sarah came to therapy believing she was, in her words, ""fundamentally broken."" She had this successful career, great friends, but inside? She felt like an impostor in her own life. Through our work together, she started to recognize how her childhood experiences of emotional neglect had created this disconnect between her actual capabilities and her sense of worth.

The breakthrough came when she realized that every time she started feeling confident, she'd unconsciously sabotage herself. Why? Because confidence felt dangerous. In her childhood home, standing out or showing pride in herself meant being cut down to size. Once she could see this pattern, she could start challenging it.

This is where the real work begins – in those small, daily moments when you catch yourself automatically defaulting to self-doubt or self-criticism. It's about creating new neural pathways, new ways of responding to yourself. And let me tell you, it's not always pretty. Sometimes it's messy, sometimes it's two steps forward and one step back, but that's okay. That's part of the process.

What matters is that you're here, doing the work, showing up for yourself maybe for the first time in your life. And speaking of showing up for yourself, I want you to pay attention to something: that voice inside that's been saying ""this won't work for me"" or ""I'm too broken"" while you've been listening? That's not your voice. That's the voice of trauma, and we're going to learn how to turn down its volume.

Remember, rebuilding self-worth with CPTSD isn't about becoming a different person – it's about uncovering who you've been all along, beneath the layers of trauma responses and survival mechanisms. It's about reclaiming your right to take up space in the world, to have needs, to make mistakes, to be gloriously, imperfectly human.

Let's pause here for a moment and let that sink in, because what comes next is where we start getting really practical about how to put these insights into action. And trust me, you're going to want to hear this...

You know what's fascinating? The science behind all of this actually backs up what so many of us have experienced in our healing journeys. Let me share some research that really blew my mind when I first discovered it – and I think it'll resonate with you too.

Dr. Bessel van der Kolk, who literally wrote the book on trauma with ""The Body Keeps the Score,"" found that trauma fundamentally changes how our brains process self-worth. His research shows that the areas of our brain responsible for self-perception – particularly the medial prefrontal cortex – actually show reduced activity in people with CPTSD. But here's the hopeful part: these changes aren't permanent. Our brains have this amazing ability called neuroplasticity, which means we can literally rewire those neural pathways.

I remember sitting in my therapist's office when I first learned about this, thinking ""Holy shit, you mean my brain isn't permanently broken?"" And the answer is a resounding no. A 2019 study published in the Journal of Traumatic Stress showed that targeted interventions focusing on self-worth could actually increase activity in those same brain regions within just 12 weeks. That's pretty damn impressive, right?

But here's where it gets even more interesting. Dr. Christine Courtois, who's been studying complex trauma for decades, found that self-worth reconstruction happens in stages. Her research shows that we can't just jump from self-loathing to self-love – it's more like building a house. You need the foundation first, then the walls, then the roof. And guess what? That matches exactly what many of you have been sharing in your stories.

Now, let me tell you about a study that really hits home. Researchers at the University of California conducted a long-term study following survivors of chronic childhood trauma. They found that people who successfully rebuilt their self-worth typically went through what they called the ""Triple-R Process"": Recognition, Reframing, and Reclamation. Sound familiar? It's exactly what Sarah was describing in her story earlier.

Here's something that might surprise you – a 2020 meta-analysis published in the Journal of Counseling Psychology found that self-worth recovery isn't just about positive thinking. In fact, forcing positive thoughts without addressing the underlying trauma can actually be counterproductive. It's like putting a fresh coat of paint on a crumbling wall – looks nice for a minute, but it's not fixing the structural issues.

Instead, the research points to something called ""compassionate self-awareness."" Dr. Kristin Neff's work shows that people who develop self-compassion – not just self-esteem – show more resilience and stable self-worth over time. And get this: they found that self-compassion actually changes our cortisol levels. That's right, being kind to yourself literally changes your body's stress response.

Let me share something personal here. When I first read these studies, I felt this weird mix of validation and frustration. Validation because finally, here was scientific proof of what we've been experiencing. Frustration because, well, why the hell didn't anyone tell us this sooner? But that's exactly why we're talking about it now.

The research also shows something really important about shame – you know, that lovely little companion that likes to tell us we're not worthy. Dr. Brené Brown's research demonstrates that shame resilience is actually a learnable skill. Her studies show that people who develop shame resilience aren't immune to feeling shame (wouldn't that be nice?), but they recover from it faster and it has less power over their self-worth.

Here's a mind-bending finding from a 2021 study in the Journal of Trauma & Dissociation: our self-worth actually has a physical component. They found that trauma survivors who engaged in regular body-based practices – like yoga or dance – showed significant improvements in their sense of self-worth compared to those who only did talk therapy. It's like our bodies need to learn we're worthy just as much as our minds do.

But perhaps the most encouraging research comes from the field of post-traumatic growth. Studies by Dr. Richard Tedeschi and Dr. Lawrence Calhoun show that many trauma survivors don't just recover – they actually develop stronger self-worth than they had before. They call it ""stress-induced growth,"" but I like to think of it as our emotional immune system getting stronger after fighting off an infection.

Now, here's where the science gets really practical. A 2022 longitudinal study followed CPTSD survivors for five years and found that the most successful recovery journeys had three common elements: consistent support systems, gradual exposure to healthy relationships, and regular practice of self-advocacy. In other words, we can't do this alone, and we shouldn't have to.

The research also validates something many of us have felt intuitively – recovery isn't linear. Dr. Judith Herman's updated work shows that healing from complex trauma follows more of a spiral pattern, where we revisit similar themes but from progressively stronger positions. So those times when you feel like you're going backwards? That's actually a normal part of the process.

You know what really gets me excited? The emerging research on neurogenesis – our brain's ability to create new neurons throughout our lives. Studies show that every time we practice self-compassion or challenge a negative self-belief, we're literally growing new neural pathways. We're not just healing; we're evolving.

Let me wrap up this research section with something powerful: a 2023 study found that understanding the science behind trauma recovery actually improved outcomes for survivors. Just knowing that there's solid research backing up our experiences, understanding that our struggles are valid and our healing is possible – that knowledge itself becomes part of our recovery toolkit.

And speaking of tools, that's exactly where we're heading next. Because while all this research is fascinating, what really matters is how we can use it in our daily lives to rebuild our sense of worth...

You know what's fascinating about rebuilding self-worth after CPTSD? It's like renovating a house that's still standing, but the foundation needs serious work. And here's where it gets really interesting – we often try to fix the paint and decor first, when what we really need is to shore up those foundational beliefs about ourselves.

Let me share something that blew my mind when I first learned it: our self-worth isn't actually built on achievements or other people's approval – though God knows many of us tried that route, right?  No, it's built on this radical concept of inherent value. And I say ""radical"" because for those of us with CPTSD, believing we have inherent worth feels about as natural as tap-dancing on Jupiter.

Now, here's where things get juicy. When we're dealing with developmental trauma, our brain literally got wired to question our worth at every turn. It's like we've got this internal critic on steroids, and it's working overtime to ""protect"" us by keeping us small. The kicker? That critic thinks it's doing us a favor. Wild, right?

But check this out – and this is something I wish someone had told me years ago – the path to rebuilding self-worth isn't about fighting that critic. It's about understanding it, then gradually showing it that the danger has passed. Think of it like taming a very scared, very aggressive guard dog. You don't yell at it for barking; you show it that the world is safer than it thinks.

Let's go deeper into this, because this is where so many of us get stuck. We've internalized this idea that we're somehow fundamentally flawed, and we keep looking for evidence to support that belief. It's like we're collecting receipts for our own unworthiness.  Sound familiar? Yeah, I thought it might.

But here's the thing that changed everything for me: what if we're not actually broken? What if all those adaptations – the people-pleasing, the perfectionism, the hypervigilance – were actually brilliant survival strategies? Because that's exactly what they were. You didn't develop these patterns because you were weak; you developed them because you were strong enough to survive some seriously tough shit.

Now, I can hear some of you thinking, ""Great, Gregory, but how does understanding this actually help me feel worthy?"" And that's exactly the right question. Because insight without action is just intellectual masturbation – feels good in the moment but doesn't actually get you anywhere. 

The real transformation happens when we start practicing what I call ""worth-building behaviors."" These aren't your typical self-help exercises – we're talking about fundamental shifts in how we relate to ourselves. It's about catching those moments when we automatically defer to others and asking, ""What would I do if I believed I mattered just as much?"" It's about noticing when we minimize our achievements and practicing sitting with pride instead of shame.

And let me tell you something crucial about this process – it feels fake as hell at first. Seriously, when I started practicing self-worth, I felt like the world's worst actor. But here's the secret: your nervous system doesn't know the difference between ""fake it till you make it"" and the real deal. It responds to practice, to repetition, to the gradual accumulation of new experiences.

Think about it like learning a new language. At first, every word feels clumsy and foreign. You're constantly translating in your head, and it's exhausting. But eventually, without even noticing when it happened, you start thinking in that new language. That's exactly what happens when we practice self-worth – we're literally learning to speak a new emotional language.

The really beautiful part? This isn't about becoming someone new. It's about uncovering who you've been all along, beneath the layers of trauma and adaptation. It's like archaeology of the self, brushing away the dirt to reveal the precious artifacts that were always there.

And yes, there will be setbacks. Days when that inner critic is screaming so loud you can barely hear yourself think. Days when you fall back into old patterns of self-doubt and shame. But here's what I want you to remember: setbacks aren't failures. They're part of the process. They're opportunities to practice self-compassion, which, ironically, is one of the most powerful worth-building behaviors we can engage in.

You know what's really wild? As your sense of worth starts to rebuild, you'll notice changes in areas of your life you didn't even realize were connected. Boundaries become easier to set. Relationships start to shift. You'll find yourself making different choices, not because you're forcing yourself to, but because they naturally align with your growing sense of self-worth.

This is deep work, my friends. It's not always comfortable, and it's definitely not linear. But it is possible. And more than that, it's your birthright. Because despite what your trauma may have taught you, despite what those early experiences might have suggested, you are worthy. Not because of what you do or what you achieve, but simply because you exist.

And that truth? That's the foundation we're rebuilding on. Everything else – the tools, the techniques, the practices – they all stem from this core reality. Your worth isn't something you need to earn or prove. It's something you need to remember.

You know what really drives this point home for me? The stories I hear from our community. They're like individual pieces of a massive mosaic, each one showing different paths to reclaiming self-worth, and honestly? They're fucking inspiring.

Let me tell you about Sarah – and of course, that's not her real name, I always change details to protect privacy. Sarah wrote to me about growing up with a mother who measured love in achievements. A-plus or nothing, first place or don't bother coming home. She internalized this so deeply that by her thirties, she had three degrees, a corner office, and a constant, gnawing feeling that she was still somehow not enough.

""Gregory,"" she wrote, ""I had this moment in therapy where I realized I was still trying to earn my mom's gold star at forty-two years old. I was sitting there listing off all my accomplishments like items on a grocery list, and my therapist just asked, 'But what makes you happy?' And I... I just broke down because I had no fucking clue.""

Here's where it gets good though – Sarah started small. She began doing one thing each day just because she wanted to, not because it would look good on a resume or impress anyone. Some days it was watching cartoons in her pajamas. Others, it was taking a painting class where she was gloriously, absolutely terrible at it. And you know what? She started feeling more alive, more real, more... herself.

Then there's Marcus – his story hit me particularly hard because it shows how our work environments can reinforce these patterns we're trying to break. He spent fifteen years in a toxic corporate culture where his worth was measured in billable hours and client feedback scores. Sound familiar to anyone? Yeah, I thought so.

Marcus shared how he'd developed this habit of apologizing for everything – taking bathroom breaks, eating lunch, having a life outside work. It was like he was apologizing for existing in spaces that weren't directly productive. But the turning point? It came from an unexpected place.

""I was apologizing to my five-year-old nephew for having to take a phone call during our time together,"" he wrote, ""and this kid looks at me and says, 'Uncle Marcus, you don't have to say sorry for everything.' From the mouths of babes, right?""

That moment sparked something in Marcus. He started tracking his apologies – not to shame himself, but to understand the pattern. And holy shit, was it eye-opening. He was apologizing for things like taking up space in elevators, for having emotions, for needing help with projects. Classic CPTSD stuff, right?

Let me share another story that I think will resonate with many of you. Jamie – who wrote to us from somewhere in the Pacific Northwest – described their journey as ""learning to exist without a permission slip."" Fuck, if that isn't the perfect way to put it.

They grew up in an environment where their gender expression, their creative interests, even their food preferences were constantly policed and criticized. By adulthood, they had this elaborate internal system of checks and balances for every decision, every movement, every breath. Sound exhausting? It was.

""I realized I was treating myself like a perpetual trespasser in my own life,"" Jamie wrote. ""I was constantly looking for someone to validate my right to be anywhere, do anything, feel anything. It was like I was living in a house where I had to ask permission to sit on my own furniture.""

The way Jamie started rebuilding their sense of self-worth was beautiful in its simplicity. They started with their space – their apartment. They painted one wall bright yellow. Not because it was tasteful or because anyone else would like it, but because they wanted to wake up to sunshine even on grey days. It was a fuck-you to every voice that had ever told them to make themselves smaller, more palatable, less... them.

And you know what? These stories – Sarah's, Marcus's, Jamie's – they're not extraordinary because they're rare. They're extraordinary because they're so damn common. They're variations on the same theme we all know: the journey from ""I must earn my worth"" to ""I am worthy because I exist.""

I received a letter just last week from Alex, who described what they called their ""worth revolution."" They wrote about how they used to think self-worth was like a bank account – you had to make deposits of achievements, good deeds, and perfect behavior to build it up. But life has a way of teaching us, doesn't it?

Alex got sick – the kind of sick that takes you out of the game for months. No more achievements. No more perfectly curated social media presence. No more being the reliable one, the strong one, the one who has their shit together. And in that stripped-down state, they found something profound.

""It was like the universe forced me to learn what you've been saying all along,"" they wrote. ""My worth didn't disappear when I couldn't perform anymore. It was there in the people who brought me soup, in the cat who slept on my feet, in the simple act of breathing in and out. It was always there. I just couldn't see it through all the doing.""

These stories, every single one of them, they're not just about individual journeys. They're mirrors reflecting back our own struggles and triumphs. They're reminders that this path we're on? We're not walking it alone. Every step you take toward recognizing your inherent worth echoes in the steps of others doing the same work.

And that's the thing about rebuilding self-worth – it's both intensely personal and universally human. Whether it's Sarah learning to enjoy things without grading her performance, Marcus reclaiming his right to take up space, Jamie painting their wall yellow, or Alex discovering worth in stillness – these are all different paths leading to the same truth we talked about earlier.

You know what I love about all these stories we just heard? They remind us that rebuilding self-worth isn't some fancy theoretical concept – it's about those small, brave steps we take every single day. So let me share some practical tools that have worked not just for me, but for countless survivors I've connected with over the years.

First up, let's talk about what I call the ""Permission Slip Practice."" Remember how Jamie painted their wall yellow? That's exactly the kind of thing I'm talking about. Start small – give yourself permission to do one tiny thing each day that your inner critic usually vetoes. Maybe it's wearing that bright shirt you love but think is ""too much,"" or ordering what you actually want at a restaurant instead of the cheapest thing on the menu. Here's the crucial part: when that critical voice pipes up, acknowledge it with ""I hear you, and I'm choosing to do this anyway.""

Now, something that's been absolutely game-changing for many of us is what I've come to call ""Evidence Collection."" Our trauma-trained brains are fantastic at gathering evidence for why we suck – let's use that same skill but flip the script. Every night, write down three things you did that required strength, kindness, or competence. They don't have to be huge – maybe you made your bed, sent that scary email, or helped someone find something at the grocery store. Over time, you're building a concrete case file that challenges that ""I'm worthless"" narrative.

Here's where it gets really interesting – and stick with me on this one because it might sound a bit wild at first. We're going to practice ""Future Self Conversations."" Think about yourself five years from now, having done all this healing work. What would that version of you say to current you? I know, I know, it sounds cheesy as hell, but there's something powerful about stepping outside our current pain and accessing that wider perspective. Your future self knows you survive this. They know you're doing the best you can with what you've got right now.

Let's get physical for a moment – and no, I'm not about to break into an Olivia Newton-John routine, though hey, that might not be a bad healing tool either! I'm talking about using our bodies to reinforce our worth. Try this: stand in front of a mirror (I know, stay with me here), put your hand on your heart, and say one kind thing to yourself. If that feels too intense – and believe me, I get it – start by just placing your hand on your heart and breathing. That's it. You're literally showing up for yourself in a physical way.

Something that's been particularly helpful for folks dealing with perfectionism – and isn't that most of us with CPTSD? – is what I call the ""Good Enough Revolution."" Set a timer for 20 minutes and do something imperfectly on purpose. Draw a wonky picture, write a messy first draft, fold your laundry ""badly."" The goal isn't to do it wrong – it's to prove to yourself that good enough is, well, good enough.

Here's another practice that's been transformative for many of us: the ""Younger Self Check-In."" When you're making decisions or setting boundaries, ask yourself, ""What would little Gregory (or whatever your name is) need right now?"" Sometimes, just that shift in perspective can help us access the compassion we so readily offer others but struggle to give ourselves.

One tool that's particularly useful when we're spiraling into shame is the ""Fact vs. Story"" technique. When you catch yourself thinking ""I'm such a failure,"" pause and separate the facts from the story. Fact: ""I made a mistake at work."" Story: ""I'm a complete failure who'll never succeed at anything."" See the difference? This isn't about toxic positivity – it's about accuracy.

Look, I know these tools might seem simple, maybe even obvious to some of you. But here's the thing about healing from CPTSD – it's not usually the complex strategies that create lasting change. It's the simple practices we return to again and again, like water slowly reshaping rock. It's about creating new neural pathways, and that happens through consistent, gentle practice.

Remember, you don't have to do all of these at once. Pick one that resonates and start there. Maybe it's just the hand-on-heart thing for now. That's perfect. Because rebuilding self-worth isn't about grand gestures – it's about these small moments of choosing ourselves, over and over again, until it starts to feel natural.

In a minute, we'll dive into some additional tools that build on these foundations, but first, I want to emphasize something crucial: there's no timeline for this work. You're not behind, you're not doing it wrong, and you're exactly where you need to be right now.

So here's where things get really interesting - and I mean that in the most practical way possible. You know how we just talked about those foundational pieces? Well, now I want to share some next-level tools that have been absolute game-changers for both me and many of my clients.

One powerful technique I absolutely love is what I call the ""Evidence Journal."" Now, don't roll your eyes at me - I know how that sounds, but stick with me here. When our self-worth has been hammered by trauma, our brains become exceptional at collecting evidence that we're not enough. This journal flips that script. Every day, you write down one tiny piece of evidence that challenges your negative self-belief. And I mean tiny - maybe you remembered to water your plants, or you sent that text you were avoiding, or hell, you just got out of bed. That counts.

What makes this particularly powerful - and this is the part that blew my mind when I first discovered it - is that you're actually rewiring your brain's filtering system. You're teaching it to notice and catalog the good stuff instead of just the shit that confirms your trauma-based beliefs. It's like installing a new operating system, but instead of Windows updates that crash your computer, this one actually works.

Here's another tool that's been revolutionary for many of us: the ""Future Self Dialogue."" Now, I know this might sound a bit woo-woo, but hear me out. Imagine yourself five years from now, having done all this healing work. This version of you has developed solid self-worth. When you're facing a particularly rough moment, you can actually have a conversation with this future self. What would they tell you? What wisdom would they share? It's surprisingly powerful because it helps you access parts of yourself that already know you're worthy - they're just buried under layers of trauma response.

Let me share something personal here. When I first started doing this work, I thought it was complete bullshit. Like, talking to my future self? Come on. But one day, when I was in a particularly dark place, I tried it. And you know what my future self said? He just laughed and said, ""Oh man, I remember this part. It gets so much better."" That simple moment shifted something fundamental for me.

Now, here's a tool that's particularly useful for those moments when you're feeling overwhelmed by shame or self-doubt - what I call the ""Compassion Swap."" When you catch yourself in negative self-talk, pause and ask yourself: ""Would I say this to someone else who's been through what I've been through?"" The answer is almost always no. Then, take whatever compassionate response you would offer to another person and direct it toward yourself. It feels weird at first, like wearing someone else's shoes, but it gets more natural with practice.

Something that pairs really well with this is what I call ""Permission Slips."" And yes, I literally mean writing yourself permission slips. Permission to rest, to make mistakes, to not have it all figured out. Permission to take up space, to have needs, to set boundaries. It might seem silly, but there's something powerful about physically writing out ""I give myself permission to..."" It's like your brain goes, ""Oh, wait, this is official now.""

Let's talk about something that's often overlooked - the role of the body in rebuilding self-worth. Trauma lives in our bodies, and sometimes the fastest way to shift our sense of worth is through physical practices. This could be as simple as standing in what Amy Cuddy calls a ""power pose"" for two minutes, or as involved as regular movement practices that help you reclaim your relationship with your body.

The key with all these tools is to approach them with curiosity rather than pressure. Think of them as experiments. Some will work brilliantly for you, others might not resonate at all, and that's perfectly okay. Your healing journey is uniquely yours, and part of building self-worth is trusting your own experience of what works for you.

Remember, you're not just doing these practices to feel better in the moment - though that's certainly valuable. You're actually rewiring neural pathways that were shaped by trauma. Each time you use one of these tools, you're laying down new neural tracks, creating alternative pathways to the ones carved by trauma. It's like you're building a whole new network of roads in your brain, and over time, these new routes become the ones you naturally take.

Let's explore how to integrate these tools into daily life in a way that feels sustainable and authentic to you...

You know what I love about this journey of rebuilding self-worth? It's that moment when you start to notice the small shifts – those tiny victories that add up to something profound. Like when you catch yourself standing a little taller in the grocery store, or speaking up in a meeting without that familiar knot in your stomach. These aren't just random occurrences; they're evidence of your nervous system rewiring itself, creating new patterns of self-belief.

I had a beautiful email from Sarah last week that really drove this home. She wrote about how she used to apologize for literally everything – even when someone else bumped into her cart at the store. But after six months of practicing the tools we've discussed, she caught herself mid-sorry and thought, ""Wait, I actually didn't do anything wrong here."" That's the kind of shit that makes me want to do a happy dance, folks. That's integration in action.

Here's what I want you to remember as you move forward: this isn't about becoming a completely different person. It's about uncovering who you've always been beneath the layers of trauma responses and protective mechanisms. Think of it like archaeological work – gentle, patient, and deeply respectful of what you find.

And speaking of gentle, let's talk about the pace of this work. I know some of you are sitting there thinking, ""Greg, I've been doing all these exercises, but I still feel like shit sometimes."" First off, welcome to the club – we meet on Thursdays and there's cookies. But seriously, healing isn't linear. Some days you'll feel like a self-worth warrior, and others you'll want to hide under your blankets and binge-watch The Great British Bake Off. Both are perfectly valid responses.

The key is to keep showing up for yourself, even when it feels like you're taking two steps forward and one step back. Remember those neural pathways we talked about? They're like footpaths in a forest – the more you walk them, the clearer and easier to follow they become. But occasionally, you'll still trip over a root or get your foot stuck in the mud. That's not failure; that's just part of the journey.

As we wrap up today's episode, I want you to take a moment to acknowledge how far you've come. Whether you're just starting to explore these concepts or you've been working on them for years, you're here. You're listening. You're doing the work. That alone is worth celebrating.

Next week, we're diving into a topic that's intimately connected to self-worth: finding peace through forgiveness. And before anyone breaks out in hives, no, this isn't about forcing yourself to forgive everyone who's hurt you. It's about understanding how forgiveness – both of others and ourselves – can be a powerful tool in our healing journey.

Until then, keep being gentle with yourself. Keep practicing these tools in ways that feel authentic to you. And remember, you're not alone in this work. There's a whole community of us out here, making sense of our shit together, one day at a time.

And she just sat there, calm as could be, and said something that changed everything for me. She said, ""Gregory, forgiveness isn't for them. It's for the part of you that's exhausted from carrying all this.""

Let that sink in for a moment. Because here's what I've learned - and what I see in so many of our community members' stories - we often confuse forgiveness with reconciliation. They're not the same thing at all. You can forgive someone and still never want to see their face again. You can forgive and still maintain those boundaries that keep you safe and sane.

Sometimes, the person we most need to forgive is ourselves. That little kid inside who didn't know how to stop the bad things from happening. That teenager who developed survival mechanisms that don't serve us anymore. That adult who's still beating themselves up for not ""getting over it"" faster.

Now, this isn't some quick fix where you just say ""I forgive"" and suddenly everything's sunshine and rainbows. It's a process, and it happens in its own time.

And you know what? Sometimes we're not ready to forgive, and that's okay too. Forcing forgiveness before you're ready is like trying to heal a broken bone before it's been properly set - it just doesn't work, and it might even cause more damage.

What I've found most helpful is starting with small acts of self-forgiveness. Maybe you forgive yourself for that awkward thing you said three years ago that still keeps you up at night. Or for not standing up to that toxic friend sooner. Baby steps, right?

The real power move? It's when you realize that forgiveness isn't about erasing the past - it's about creating space for your future. It's about saying, ""Yeah, that happened, and it sucked, and it wasn't okay, but I'm not letting it define every moment of my life going forward.""

Because here's the truth bomb - holding onto unforgiveness is like drinking poison and expecting the other person to die. And I don't know about you, but I've spent enough time poisoning myself. I'm ready to put down that cup and pick up something that actually nourishes me.

Remember, my fellow trauma warriors, this isn't about rushing the process or forcing anything. It's about giving yourself permission to heal in whatever way feels right for you. And sometimes, that means taking the long way home. That's okay. You've got time, and you've got us right here with you.

So let's try something together right now - a gentle practice that might help you start exploring forgiveness in a way that feels safe and manageable. I want you to get comfortable wherever you are, and if you're driving or can't close your eyes, that's totally cool - just soften your gaze a bit.

Take a few breaths, and I want you to think of something really small that you're holding onto - not the big heavy stuff, we're not going there yet. Maybe it's being annoyed at yourself for forgetting to buy milk, or that time last week when you snapped at your cat. Something tiny that carries just a whisper of that self-judgment we're so good at.

Now, here's where it gets interesting - imagine that same scenario, but pretend it's your best friend telling you about it. They forgot the milk, they had a grumpy moment with their pet. Notice how your perspective probably shifts automatically. That warmth, that understanding you'd offer them without hesitation - that's what we're aiming to redirect toward ourselves.

Sit with that contrast for a moment. Feel the difference between how you judge yourself and how you'd comfort a friend. No need to force anything to change - just notice. This is what I call the ""friendship mirror,"" and it's a powerful tool for beginning to soften that harsh inner voice.

You might notice some resistance coming up - that's normal. Your brain might be throwing out thoughts like ""But I should have known better"" or ""It's different when it's me."" Just let those thoughts float by like leaves on a stream. We see them, we acknowledge them, but we don't need to grab onto them.

Try bringing in that friend-energy toward yourself, just for this one small thing. Maybe place a hand on your heart if that feels right, and whisper whatever words of kindness you'd offer to someone else. ""Hey, it's okay. You're human. These things happen.""

Notice what happens in your body as you do this. There might be tension, there might be relief, there might be nothing at all. Whatever shows up is perfectly fine. This isn't about getting it ""right"" - it's about practicing a new way of being with ourselves.

Let's stay here for just another breath or two, holding ourselves in that same gentle space we so naturally create for others. This is the seed of self-forgiveness - this simple act of extending the same grace to ourselves that we so freely give to others.

When you're ready, bring your attention back to the room, knowing you can return to this practice anytime. It might feel awkward or even silly at first - that's totally normal. Let me share another practice that's been a game-changer for so many in our community - I call it the ""Future Self Letter."" Take about fifteen minutes with this. Find a quiet spot - maybe grab your favorite beverage, whatever helps you feel grounded. Start writing, ""Dear ,"" and just let your future self speak. Sometimes I joke that my nervous system is like that overprotective parent who never lets you do anything fun – except instead of stopping me from going to parties, it's stopping me from having consistent energy levels.

Let me paint you a picture of what this often looks like. You wake up feeling decent, maybe even good. You make plans, you start tackling your day, and then suddenly – usually right when you need your energy the most – your body decides it's done. Not just ""I need a coffee"" done, but ""I might need to lie down on this floor right now"" done. And the kicker? There's often no obvious trigger. It's like your body's playing energy roulette, and you never know where that ball's gonna land.

What makes this especially tricky is that society isn't exactly set up for people with fluctuating energy levels. We're expected to maintain this consistent level of productivity, to ""push through"" and ""just do it."" But when you're dealing with CPTSD, pushing through isn't always an option – and honestly, it's not always the healthiest choice either.

I remember this one time I tried to power through when my energy was tanking. It's about understanding our unique energy patterns, learning to honor our bodies' signals, and developing strategies that work with our nervous systems instead of against them.

As we move forward in today's episode, we're going to explore this from all angles – the science behind it, practical strategies that actually work, and most importantly, how to make peace with our energy needs. Because at the end of the day, this isn't about fitting into some standardized mold of how we ""should"" function. It's about creating a sustainable way of living that honors our experiences and supports our healing journey.

So let's really dig into what's happening when our energy tanks hit empty, because this is where a lot of us get stuck in some pretty gnarly shame spirals. You know those moments - when you're lying on the couch, staring at the ceiling, and that inner critic starts up with its greatest hits: ""Everyone else can handle this,"" or ""You're just being lazy."" Yeah, I've been there too, and let me tell you something important - that voice is full of shit.

Here's what's actually going on in our bodies when we're dealing with CPTSD energy crashes. Our nervous systems have basically been running a marathon while trying to convince everyone (including ourselves) that we're just out for a casual jog. The hypervigilance alone - that constant scanning for threats that most of us don't even realize we're doing - burns through energy like you wouldn't believe. It's like having fifty browser tabs open in your brain, all running complex programs, all the time.

Now, something I noticed in my own journey - and I see this with so many of my clients - is that we often try to push through these energy dips like we're still in survival mode. It's this weird paradox where we're using survival strategies to try to heal from survival mode. Makes about as much sense as trying to put out a fire with gasoline, right?

Let me share something that happened to me last week. I had this perfectly planned day - you know, the kind where you think you've finally got your shit together. But by noon, I could feel that familiar energy crash coming on. The old me would have powered through, probably with enough coffee to give a rhino the jitters. Instead, I did something that still feels radical: I listened to my body. I took a twenty-minute rest - not even a nap, just quiet time with my eyes closed. And you know what? The world didn't end. In fact, I got more done in the afternoon than I typically would forcing myself to push through.

This brings me to something really important about energy management with CPTSD - it's not just about physical energy. We're dealing with emotional energy, mental energy, and what I like to call ""people energy."" That last one's especially tricky for those of us with trauma backgrounds because social interactions often require extra processing power. It's like running your phone's GPS, camera, and three social media apps all at once - your battery's gonna drain faster than normal.

But here's where it gets interesting - and I really want you to hear this - your energy needs aren't a character flaw. They're not something to fix or overcome. They're valuable information from your body about what you need to feel safe and functional. When we start treating these signals with curiosity instead of judgment, something remarkable happens: we start developing a more nuanced understanding of our own rhythms.

Think of it like this: if you were caring for a small child who was exhausted, would you berate them for needing rest? Would you force them to keep going until they collapsed? Of course not. Yet somehow, we've internalized this idea that showing ourselves the same basic compassion is somehow wrong or weak.

Let's talk about what I call the ""energy debt cycle."" It's this pattern where we push ourselves way past our limits, crash hard, feel shame about crashing, then try to make up for lost time by pushing even harder. Sound familiar? Yeah, it's about as sustainable as trying to pay off one credit card with another. The interest just keeps compounding until we're emotionally bankrupt.

The shift happens when we start working with our energy patterns instead of against them. This isn't about lowering our standards or giving up on our goals - it's about being strategic with the energy we have. Sometimes that means doing less but doing it more consistently. Sometimes it means scheduling rest before we need it, which feels counterintuitive as hell when you're used to running on empty.

I had a client who used to pride herself on working twelve-hour days, until she realized she was spending her weekends in a dissociative fog just trying to recover. When she started building in regular breaks and actually honoring her body's needs, her overall productivity actually increased. More importantly, she started feeling more present in her own life.

The thing is, managing energy with CPTSD isn't just about finding the right productivity hack or wellness routine. It's about fundamentally changing our relationship with rest and activity. It's about understanding that our needs might look different from other people's needs, and that's okay. It's about recognizing that sometimes the most productive thing we can do is absolutely nothing.

And let me tell you, this isn't some quick fix situation. It's more like learning a new language - at first, everything feels awkward and confusing, and you make a lot of mistakes. But gradually, you start to understand the subtle signals your body's been trying to send you all along. You begin to recognize the difference between productive tiredness and trauma exhaustion. You learn to spot the early warning signs of energy depletion before you hit crisis mode.

This is where the real healing happens - not in pushing ourselves harder, but in learning to work with our natural rhythms and needs. It's about building a sustainable relationship with our energy that supports our healing instead of depleting it.

So here's where the science gets really fascinating, my friends. When we look at the research around energy management and CPTSD, we're seeing some groundbreaking studies that validate what many of us have experienced firsthand. Dr. Bessel van der Kolk's work, particularly in ""The Body Keeps the Score,"" shows us how trauma literally changes our body's energy systems at a cellular level. It's not just in our heads – our bodies are actually working differently.

Let me share something that blew my mind when I first learned about it. A 2019 study published in the Journal of Affective Disorders found that people with CPTSD have significantly altered cortisol patterns compared to those without trauma histories. Think about that for a second. Our stress hormone system – you know, the one that's supposed to give us energy when we need it and help us rest when we don't – it's operating on a completely different schedule. No wonder we sometimes feel like we're running on empty when others seem fine.

And here's where it gets even more interesting. Research from the National Institute of Mental Health shows that trauma survivors often experience what they call ""allostatic load"" – basically, the wear and tear on your body from chronic stress. It's like running your car in the red zone all the time. The engine might keep going, but something's eventually gonna give.

 

You know, sometimes when I'm sharing this research, I can feel people thinking, ""Great, Gregory, more bad news."" But hold on – here's where hope comes in. A 2020 study in the Journal of Traumatic Stress showed that implementing regular energy management techniques – like the ones we talked about earlier – can actually help regulate these systems over time. Our bodies have this amazing capacity to heal and adapt.

Dr. Stephen Porges – yeah, the polyvagal theory guy – his research shows how we can actually train our nervous system to better regulate energy. It's like having a thermostat that's been stuck on high, and slowly learning to adjust it back to a comfortable temperature. The science suggests that consistent, gentle practices are more effective than trying to force massive changes all at once.

Now, I want to share something really exciting from a 2021 study in Frontiers in Psychology. They found that trauma survivors who implemented regular ""energy check-ins"" – basically, what we've been talking about with monitoring our battery levels – showed significant improvements in their daily functioning within just eight weeks. Eight weeks! That's not a lifetime; that's like two months of Netflix subscription.

But here's the kicker – and this is important – the same study showed that the participants who tried to push through their fatigue actually experienced setbacks. The research confirms what we've been saying: forcing yourself to ""power through"" isn't just unhelpful; it's counterproductive.

You know what really gets me excited? The emerging research on chronobiology and trauma. Scientists are finding that each of us has unique energy patterns – they call it our ""chronotype."" When we work with these natural patterns instead of against them, we see better outcomes in trauma healing. It's like finally reading the user manual for your own body.

A fascinating study from the University of Michigan showed that trauma survivors who aligned their high-energy tasks with their natural peak times reported 40% better outcomes in their therapy work. Forty percent! That's not a small number, friends. That's the difference between feeling like you're drowning and feeling like you might actually get your head above water.

And let's talk about something that doesn't get enough attention – the research on social energy and trauma. A 2022 study in the Journal of Psychiatric Research found that trauma survivors often spend up to three times more energy in social situations than others. No wonder we feel wiped out after what others consider ""simple"" social interactions. It's not in your head – you're literally using more energy.

Here's something particularly relevant for our community: research from the Complex Trauma Foundation shows that implementing energy management strategies can reduce dissociative episodes by up to 60%. That's huge. When we honor our energy needs, we're not just feeling better – we're actually changing how our nervous system responds to stress.

The science is clear on one thing: sustainable energy management isn't about pushing harder or doing more. It's about working smarter with the energy we have. Studies consistently show that small, consistent adjustments are more effective than dramatic overhauls that we can't maintain.

And you know what? This research validates what many of us have felt intuitively. When the studies show that trauma survivors need more recovery time between activities, it's not because we're weak – it's because our systems are working overtime to process and heal. Understanding this can help us be more compassionate with ourselves and make choices that actually support our healing journey.

Remember, these studies aren't just abstract numbers – they're roadmaps showing us how to work with our bodies instead of against them. They're telling us that when we honor our energy needs, we're not being lazy or difficult – we're being scientifically sound in our approach to healing.

Now, here's where things get really interesting – and I want to share something that blew my mind when I first understood it. You know how we've been talking about energy management? Well, there's this fascinating connection between our energy levels and what I like to call our ""trauma clock.""

Think about it like this: Many of us with CPTSD have spent years, maybe decades, operating on someone else's schedule. Whether it was an unpredictable parent, an demanding partner, or a toxic work environment, we learned to override our natural rhythms to survive. And let me tell you, that shit takes a toll.

I remember this one time – and I'm laughing about it now – when I tried to force myself to be a ""morning person"" because that's what successful people supposedly do, right? Holy shit, was that a disaster. My body was literally screaming ""No, dude, this isn't your rhythm!"" But I kept pushing because I thought that's what healing looked like.

Here's the deeper truth I've discovered: Our energy patterns are often directly linked to our trauma responses. Some of us might experience energy spikes in the evening because that's when we had to be most vigilant as kids. Others might crash in the afternoon because that's when we used to hide to stay safe. These aren't random patterns – they're adaptive responses that served a purpose.

But here's where it gets really powerful – and I want you to really hear this part. Once we understand these patterns, we can start working with them instead of against them. It's like having a secret decoder ring for your energy levels. And no, I'm not suggesting we stay trapped in these trauma-based patterns forever, but understanding them is the first step to transforming them.

Let me share something that happened with one of my clients – we'll call her Sarah. She used to beat herself up constantly because she couldn't maintain consistent energy throughout the day. She'd have these intense productive bursts followed by what she called ""zombie hours."" Through our work together, we discovered that her energy dips corresponded exactly to the times when, as a child, she had to be ""invisible"" to avoid her father's rage. Mind-blowing, right?

The real breakthrough came when she stopped fighting these patterns and started planning around them. She scheduled her most demanding tasks during her natural energy peaks and used her lower-energy periods for gentle, nurturing activities. And you know what? Her overall energy actually improved because she wasn't constantly fighting against her body's natural rhythm.

This brings me to something I call the ""Energy Recovery Protocol"" – and no, it's not some fancy trademarked system. I've noticed in my own journey that some days I have what I call ""trauma weather"" – those days when my energy seems to be affected by invisible storms from the past. So now that we've heard these powerful stories of people finding their way, let's dig into some practical tools you can start experimenting with today. Now, let's talk about what I call ""energy boundaries."" These are different from regular boundaries because they're specifically about protecting your energy reserves. Think of it like having a phone battery that charges differently than other people's. Maybe yours needs more frequent charging, or maybe it drains faster in certain situations. That's not a defect – it's just how your system works.

One practical way to implement energy boundaries is through what I call the ""Sacred No."" This isn't about being mean or unhelpful – it's about being honest about your energy capacity. When someone asks you to do something, give yourself permission to pause and check your energy forecast. This involves creating small rituals at the beginning and end of energy-demanding activities. Now, I want to emphasize something really important here: implementing these tools isn't about becoming some sort of energy management superhero. One thing that's been really helpful for many folks in our community is what I call ""Energy Mapping."" Take a week and just notice when your energy naturally rises and falls throughout the day. So, let's dig into some additional tools that have really helped my clients - and honestly, myself - with managing those energy fluctuations we've been talking about. You know what's fascinating? Sometimes the most effective tools are the ones that seem almost too simple at first glance.

So let's flip that natural ability on its head and make it work for our healing instead.

I remember when I first started practicing intentional visualization, I was skeptical as hell. Like, how is sitting here imagining my ""safe place"" going to help when my nervous system is lighting up like Times Square on New Year's Eve? But here's the wild thing – our brains don't actually know the difference between a vividly imagined experience and a real one. That's why nightmares can leave us sweating and shaking, right?

The game-changer for me was understanding that visualization isn't about forcing yourself to think happy thoughts or pretending everything's fine. It's more like... imagine you're rehearsing for a play, but the play is your own healing journey. You're not denying the existence of the hard stuff; you're just practicing different ways to move through it.

Let me share something that happened just last week. I was working with a client – we'll call her Sarah – who struggled with feeling physically present during therapy sessions. Her go-to response was to dissociate, which, hey, totally makes sense given her history. We started working with a simple visualization where she imagined roots growing from her feet into the earth, just like a tree. Nothing fancy, right? But after a few sessions of practicing this, she noticed she could actually feel her feet on the ground during our conversations. That's the power of this stuff – it creates new neural pathways that become available when we need them most.

Here's where it gets really interesting though. Visualization isn't just about creating peaceful scenes or safe spaces – although those are incredibly valuable tools. It can also be about reimagining our relationship with our trauma responses. Like, what if instead of seeing your hypervigilance as the enemy, you visualized it as an overworked security guard who's just trying to keep you safe? It might sound a bit woo-woo, but this kind of reframing can create profound shifts in how we relate to our symptoms.

And let's be real – some days, visualization might feel impossible. Your mind might be racing so fast it feels like trying to catch lightning in a bottle. That's okay. That's actually really important information about where you're at right now. Maybe on those days, you start with just visualizing your next breath, or the feeling of your hands resting in your lap. Sometimes the smallest steps create the biggest openings.

What I love most about visualization is that it gives us back some agency in our healing process. When so much of trauma is about feeling powerless, having this tool – this ability to actively participate in rewiring our nervous systems – that's revolutionary stuff right there.

And hey, if you're sitting there thinking ""Greg, my mind is way too chaotic for this,"" I hear you. I really do. That's exactly why we start small, why we anchor these practices in our body's wisdom, and why we always, always respect our own timing. Because this isn't about forcing anything – it's about gently opening doors to new possibilities, one visualization at a time.

 So as we sit with this, I want you to know that whatever your experience with visualization has been so far – whether it's been helpful, challenging, or somewhere in between – you're exactly where you need to be. Your journey is your own, and these tools are here to serve you, not the other way around.

Alright, let's put some of this into practice together. I want to guide you through a gentle visualization exercise that I personally find really grounding - and don't worry if your mind wanders or if you're not seeing crystal-clear images. That's totally normal and actually part of the process.

Find a comfortable position, whether you're driving, walking, or sitting somewhere quiet. If it feels safe to close your eyes, go ahead - but keeping them open is perfectly fine too. Take a slow, easy breath, whatever that means for your body right now.

I want you to imagine you're holding a small object - something simple like a leaf or a smooth stone. Notice I'm not telling you what it should be. Your nervous system knows what feels right. Maybe it's something that reminds you of a time you felt peaceful, or maybe it's just something that caught your attention today. 

Take a moment to really feel the weight of this object in your hand. Notice its temperature - is it cool like a stone from a stream, or warm like it's been sitting in the sun? How does the texture feel against your skin? There's no rush here. We're just getting curious about these sensations.

Now, and this is where it gets interesting - imagine this object has the ability to absorb any difficult emotions you're carrying. Maybe it's anxiety, maybe it's that heavy feeling of shame we know so well with CPTSD. Whatever it is, visualize it slowly moving from your body into this object. Some folks see it as a color, others feel it more as a sensation - trust whatever comes up for you.

Here's the thing that makes this practice different - you're in complete control. If at any point this feels too intense, imagine setting the object down. You can pick it up again when you're ready, or leave it be. This is your visualization, your rules.

Take another breath, and if you're still holding your object, imagine placing it somewhere safe. Maybe it's in a stream that carries those difficult emotions away, or maybe you bury it in soft earth where those feelings can transform into something new. Again, trust what feels right for your system.

Before we wrap this up, notice how your body feels now. Maybe there's a slight shift, maybe things feel the same - both are perfectly okay. This practice isn't about dramatic transformations; it's about building a gentle relationship with our inner world.

Remember, you can come back to this visualization anytime. Make it your own. Change the object, change the setting, adjust the pace - whatever serves you best. That's the beauty of this tool - it's completely adaptable to your needs in any given moment.

And if this particular practice didn't quite land for you today? That's absolutely fine. We've got plenty more tools to explore together, and finding what works for you is part of the journey.

So let's explore another visualization practice that I absolutely love - one that's helped me and countless others create a sense of safety when we're feeling overwhelmed. I call it the ""Container of Light,"" and trust me, it's not as woo-woo as it might sound.

You know those moments when everything feels like too much? When memories or emotions are spilling over and you just need some breathing room? That's exactly where this practice comes in handy. And the best part? You can do it anywhere, anytime, without anyone even knowing.

Here's what I've found works well - and remember, you can totally modify this to fit what feels right for you. Start by imagining a gentle, warm light surrounding your body. Some folks picture it like a protective bubble, others like a cocoon of golden light. I personally imagine it like one of those sci-fi force fields, because hey, who doesn't want to feel like they've got some kick-ass personal shields, right?

Now, this is where it gets interesting - and I want you to really play with this part. Imagine this light has specific qualities. Maybe it's like a filter that lets in all the good stuff - support, love, understanding - while keeping out what doesn't serve you right now. Some of my clients imagine it as having a texture - smooth, soft, or even slightly sparkly. Whatever feels protective and comforting to you.

The real magic happens when you start using this container as a boundary tool. When you're in a triggering situation - maybe at work, or during a challenging family dinner - you can quietly activate this visualization. It's like having an invisible emotional hazmat suit, if you will. And yes, I know that's a weird image, but weird works sometimes, doesn't it?

What I love most about this practice is how it combines the power of imagination with our very real need for safety. It's like giving your nervous system a gentle hug while also creating a practical tool for boundary-setting. And let's be honest - many of us with CPTSD didn't exactly get a master class in boundaries growing up.

Take your time exploring this one. Some days it might feel really strong and solid, other days it might be more like a gentle mist - and that's perfectly okay. The key is to make it yours. Play with different colors, textures, or even add some movement to it if that feels right. Remember, we're not going for some perfect visualization here - we're creating a personal tool that works for you in your healing journey.

You know what I'm realizing as we wrap up today? All this visualization stuff we've been talking about - it's kind of like learning to ride a bike. At first, you might feel wobbly and uncertain, maybe even fall a few times, but that's just part of finding your balance. And just like riding a bike, once you get it, it becomes this incredible tool for freedom and exploration.

Here's what I want you to take away from our conversation today: start small. Maybe begin with just thirty seconds of visualizing your safe space while you're brushing your teeth. That's it. No pressure to make it perfect or profound. Just thirty seconds of imagining somewhere that feels good to you. It could be as simple as picturing yourself petting a cat or feeling sunshine on your face.

I've noticed in my own healing journey that the smallest steps often lead to the biggest breakthroughs. And you know what? Sometimes those breakthroughs come when we least expect them - like when we're just hanging out with our visualization practice, not trying to force anything.

Remember how we talked about making this practice your own? That's really what integration is all about. It's taking these tools we've discussed and weaving them naturally into your daily life in a way that feels authentic to you. No need to turn your whole routine upside down - just find those little moments where visualization can slip in naturally.

And hey, if you try this and it feels weird or doesn't quite click at first, that's totally normal. You're not doing it wrong. You're just learning a new language - the language of your own healing. Take it at your own pace, be gentle with yourself, and know that every tiny step counts.
