You know, when I first started working on my own emotional stability, I felt like I was trying to build a house of cards in a windstorm. Every time I'd get a few layers stacked, something would come along and blow it all to hell. And let me tell you, that feeling of constantly starting over? It's exhausting as fuck.

I remember this one particularly rough day when I was sitting in my therapist's office, probably looking like I'd been hit by an emotional truck, and she said something that really stuck with me. She said, ""Gregory, stability isn't about never wobbling - it's about learning to catch your balance faster each time."" That hit me right in the feels, because I'd been beating myself up for not being ""fixed"" yet.

Here's what I've learned since then - and what I really want to share with you today: emotional stability in recovery isn't some magical destination where you never feel overwhelmed or triggered again. It's more like developing emotional muscle memory. Just like you don't get physically strong by lifting weights once, you don't build emotional stability overnight.

The thing is, most of us with CPTSD grew up in environments where stability was about as common as a unicorn riding a skateboard. Our nervous systems basically got programmed to expect chaos. So when things are actually calm, it can feel weird as hell - even uncomfortable. Anyone else find themselves almost creating drama when things get too peaceful? Yeah, I see you. I've been there too.

But here's where it gets interesting - and honestly, kind of hopeful. Our brains have this amazing ability called neuroplasticity. It's like having a mental Etch-a-Sketch that we can actually redraw. The patterns that got etched into our brains during trauma? They're not set in stone. We can create new patterns, new pathways, new ways of responding to life's shit storms.

I was thinking about this the other day when I was doing something as simple as making my morning coffee. There was a time when even a small setback - like running out of filters - would have sent me into a spiral of frustration and shame. Now, most days, I can handle these little hiccups without feeling like the world is ending. That's stability in action, folks. It's not about never getting frustrated - it's about having a bigger emotional buffer zone.

Let me paint you a picture of what we're actually talking about when we say ""emotional stability."" Imagine you're standing on one of those balance boards - you know, the ones that wobble back and forth. At first, you're all over the place, maybe even falling off. But with practice, you start to find your center. You learn how to make smaller adjustments instead of wild overcorrections. That's exactly what we're working toward with emotional stability.

And just like with that balance board, everyone's journey to finding their center looks different. Some of us might need to start by just standing near the board, getting comfortable with the idea of stability. Others might be ready to try some fancy moves. Both approaches are perfectly valid, and both deserve respect and recognition.

What really gets me excited about this topic - and why I wanted to dedicate an entire episode to it - is that building emotional stability is something tangible we can work on. It's not some vague concept that only exists in self-help books. It's a skill we can develop, like learning to cook or ride a bike. Sure, we might skin our knees a few times along the way, but that's part of the process.

I think what trips a lot of us up is this idea that we should somehow naturally know how to be emotionally stable. Like it's something we should have figured out by now. But here's the truth bomb I want to drop: if you grew up in an environment where emotional stability wasn't modeled or was actively disrupted, how the hell would you know what it looks like? That's like expecting someone to speak fluent French when they've only ever heard German.

So as we dive deeper into this topic today, I want you to remember something crucial: wherever you are in your journey toward emotional stability is exactly where you need to be right now. Whether you're just realizing this is something you want to work on, or you've been at it for years, you're right on time for your own healing journey.

In the next part of our conversation, we're going to look at some real-world examples of what building emotional stability actually looks like in practice. Because let's face it - theory is great, but we need to know what this stuff looks like in the messy reality of everyday life.

So here's what I've noticed after working with hundreds of survivors - building emotional stability isn't some magical switch we flip. It's more like learning to ride a bike, where we start with training wheels and gradually build our confidence. And just like riding a bike, we're probably going to wobble and fall a few times before we get our balance. That's not just okay - it's actually part of the process.

Let me share something that happened to me last week that really illustrates this. I was in the grocery store, and someone's cart accidentally bumped into mine. Now, years ago, that would have sent me into a spiral of fight-or-flight responses - my heart racing, hands shaking, ready to either snap at them or bolt from the store. But this time, I noticed something different. Sure, I felt that initial jolt of anxiety, but I was able to pause, take a breath, and recognize it for what it was - just a simple accident.

That's what we're really talking about when we discuss emotional stability - it's not about never feeling triggered or anxious. It's about developing what I like to call our ""emotional shock absorbers."" Think about it like this: when you're driving down a bumpy road, your car's shock absorbers don't eliminate the bumps - they make them manageable. They give you enough control to navigate safely.

Now, I know some of you might be thinking, ""Greg, that's great for you, but my reactions feel completely out of control."" Trust me, I get it. When we're dealing with CPTSD, our nervous system is like a car with shot shock absorbers - every little bump feels like we're going to fly off the road. But here's the beautiful thing about our brains: they're incredibly adaptable. We can actually build new neural pathways that help us respond rather than react.

Let's talk about what this looks like in practice. One of my clients - we'll call her Sarah - used to have intense emotional reactions whenever her partner raised their voice, even slightly. Growing up in a household where raised voices always led to violence, her brain had learned that any increase in volume meant danger. The problem was, her partner would sometimes get excited and speak louder when talking about something they were passionate about, with zero aggressive intent.

Sarah started building her emotional stability by first learning to recognize her triggers. She noticed the physical sensations - the tightness in her chest, the urge to flee - when her partner's voice got louder. That awareness was the first step. Then, we worked on creating what I call ""pause points"" - little moments of conscious choice between the trigger and the reaction.

Here's where it gets really interesting. Sarah started using a simple technique I call the ""Reality Check Triangle."" When triggered, she would ask herself three questions: ""Am I safe right now?"", ""What evidence do I have about the current situation?"", and ""What would my wise self say about this?"" These questions became her emotional shock absorbers, giving her brain time to process the present reality rather than reacting from past trauma.

And you know what? It wasn't perfect at first. There were times when she still reacted from that trauma place, and that's completely normal. Building emotional stability isn't about getting it right every time - it's about gradually expanding our window of tolerance and building trust in our ability to handle difficult emotions.

I want to emphasize something really important here: this work requires immense self-compassion. We're essentially rewiring responses that our brains developed to keep us safe. Our nervous systems aren't broken - they're doing exactly what they learned to do to protect us. Now we're teaching them new ways to keep us safe that better fit our current reality.

Think about it like updating the operating system on your computer. The old system isn't bad - it did its job! But now we need something that can handle our current needs better. And just like with software updates, sometimes there are glitches and crashes during the process. That's not failure - that's just part of the installation process.

What I've found most powerful in my own journey, and what I've seen work for countless others, is focusing on small, consistent steps rather than trying to overhaul everything at once. Maybe today it's just about noticing when you're getting triggered without trying to change anything. Maybe tomorrow it's about practicing one deep breath before responding. These might seem like tiny steps, but they're actually laying the foundation for lasting change.

Remember, emotional stability isn't about never feeling strong emotions - it's about building the capacity to hold those emotions without being completely overwhelmed by them. It's about creating enough internal space to choose how we want to respond rather than being at the mercy of our automatic reactions.

And here's something that still amazes me: every time we practice these skills, even in small ways, we're literally rewiring our brains. Each time we pause before reacting, each time we show ourselves compassion after a difficult moment, we're strengthening those neural pathways that support emotional stability. It's like we're gradually building a more resilient emotional infrastructure, one moment of awareness at a time.

Now, I want to dive into some fascinating research that really validates what we've been talking about. You know how I'm always saying that healing isn't just this abstract concept? Well, science is backing us up here in some pretty incredible ways.

Let me tell you about this groundbreaking study from Dr. Bessel van der Kolk's team. In their research, published in the Journal of Traumatic Stress, they found that trauma literally changes the way our amygdala – that's our brain's emotional alarm system – responds to stress. But here's the hopeful part: they also discovered that these changes aren't permanent. Through consistent practice of emotional regulation techniques, participants showed measurable improvements in their ability to handle emotional triggers.

And you know what's really wild? A 2019 study in Frontiers in Psychology showed that even simple mindfulness practices – we're talking just 10 minutes a day – can significantly strengthen the connection between our prefrontal cortex (that's our rational brain) and our emotional centers. It's like building a better highway between our thinking and feeling parts.

I remember reading this research and thinking, ""Holy shit, this is exactly what so many of us in the CPTSD community have been experiencing!"" The science is catching up to what we've known in our bones – that healing is possible, and it's not just wishful thinking.

Here's something that blew my mind: Dr. Stephen Porges's work on Polyvagal Theory shows that our nervous system is constantly evaluating our safety through something called ""neuroception."" When we're working on emotional stability, we're actually helping our nervous system update its threat detection software. It's like we're teaching our body, ""Hey, it's okay to feel safe now.""

But let's get real for a second – because you know me, I love when science meets practical application. A longitudinal study from the University of California found that people who practiced self-compassion exercises – similar to what we talked about earlier – showed a 43% reduction in emotional dysregulation over six months. That's not just feeling better; that's measurable change.

And here's where it gets even more interesting. Research from the field of neuroplasticity, particularly work by Dr. Norman Doidge, shows that our brains continue to form new neural pathways throughout our entire lives. This means that no matter how long you've been dealing with CPTSD, your brain can still adapt and heal. It's never too late to start building that emotional stability.

Let me share something that really resonates with our journey. A 2020 meta-analysis in the Journal of Affective Disorders looked at various approaches to building emotional stability in trauma survivors. They found that combining different strategies – like mindfulness, somatic awareness, and cognitive reframing – was significantly more effective than using just one approach. It's like we've been saying: there's no one-size-fits-all solution.

The research also shows something fascinating about timing. Studies indicate that small, consistent practices throughout the day are more effective than longer, less frequent sessions. It's exactly what we were talking about earlier – those micro-moments of awareness and regulation add up to create lasting change.

You know what else the research validates? The importance of self-pacing in recovery. A study from the International Journal of Mental Health Systems found that trauma survivors who were allowed to control the pace of their healing showed better outcomes than those pushed into rigid treatment protocols. This is why I'm always emphasizing the importance of listening to your own timing.

Here's a piece of research that really hit home for me: a 2021 study showed that people with CPTSD who developed strong emotional regulation skills were not only more resilient to future stressors but also reported improved relationships and better life satisfaction. It's like emotional stability creates this positive ripple effect through every area of our lives.

And get this – researchers at Harvard Medical School found that consistent emotional regulation practices actually change the density of gray matter in areas of the brain associated with self-awareness and compassion. We're literally growing the parts of our brain that help us stay stable and connected.

But here's what I think is most important about all this research: it confirms what many of us have felt intuitively – that healing isn't just about managing symptoms; it's about fundamentally changing our relationship with our emotions and ourselves. The science shows us that when we practice these skills, we're not just coping better; we're actually rewiring our brains for greater resilience and stability.

And you know what makes me geek out the most about all this research? It's how it validates the messy, non-linear nature of healing. The data shows that progress isn't a straight line – it's more like a spiral, where we might revisit old challenges but with new tools and greater understanding each time.

This is why I get so excited sharing this stuff with you all. It's not just theory or wishful thinking – there's solid science behind these approaches to building emotional stability. And knowing that, knowing that research supports what we're doing here? Well, that just makes me even more passionate about continuing this journey together.

You know what's fascinating about this whole emotional stability journey? The way it spirals upward. Like, each time we work on these skills, we're not just moving in circles - we're building on what came before. And that's where I want to take us deeper, because there's this beautiful complexity to it that we haven't touched on yet.

Let me share something that hit me hard during my own recovery. I used to think stability meant feeling good all the time - which, let's be real, is about as realistic as expecting my cat to suddenly start doing my taxes. What I've learned, and what I really want you to hear, is that Published emotional stability is more like being a skilled surfer. You're not trying to control the waves; you're learning to ride them with grace.

Here's where it gets really interesting - and stick with me on this because it might sound counterintuitive at first. Sometimes, building emotional stability actually requires us to become temporarily less stable. Wild, right? It's like renovating a house. There's this messy middle period where everything looks worse before it gets better. When we start facing our triggers and processing our trauma, things can feel pretty chaotic. And that's not just okay - it's actually a sign of progress.

I remember working with this concept myself and thinking, ""Well, shit, Gregory, you've really done it now - everything feels more intense, not less."" But that intensity was actually my system recalibrating, learning to feel safe with feeling. It's like turning up the brightness on your phone to see the screen better in sunlight - temporarily more intense, but ultimately more functional.

Now, here's something that doesn't get talked about enough - the role of what I call ""emotional micro-adjustments."" Think about how you learn to ride a bike. You're not making huge, dramatic movements; you're making thousands of tiny adjustments that eventually become automatic. Building emotional stability works the same way. It's not about massive transformations overnight; it's about small, consistent tweaks in how we respond to our emotions.

Let's dig into this a bit more, because I think it's crucial. When we're dealing with CPTSD, our emotional regulation system often feels like it's either switched fully on or fully off - there's no dimmer switch. But here's the thing: we can actually install that dimmer switch. It's not quick, and it's not always pretty, but it's absolutely possible.

I had this client - let's call her Sarah - who made this beautiful observation. She said, ""It's like I'm learning to whisper to my emotions instead of shouting at them."" And doesn't that just nail it? We're not trying to strong-arm our feelings into submission; we're developing a more nuanced relationship with them.

This brings me to another layer that often surprises people - the role of play in emotional stability. Now, before you roll your eyes (I see you there), hear me out. Play isn't just about having fun; it's about creating a safe space to experiment with emotional states. When we can approach our healing with a sense of curiosity and playfulness, even just a tiny bit, we're actually engaging some pretty sophisticated neurological processes.

Think about it - when was the last time you allowed yourself to be silly? To laugh at yourself? To approach your healing with a sense of adventure rather than grim determination? I'm not saying we should make light of our trauma - hell no - but finding moments of lightness in our healing journey can be incredibly stabilizing.

And this connects to something else that's really important - the concept of emotional complexity. Often, when we're working on stability, we can fall into the trap of thinking we should only feel one thing at a time. But Published emotional stability actually allows for multiple feelings to coexist. You can be both grateful for your progress and frustrated with your setbacks. You can love someone and be angry with them. This capacity to hold seemingly contradictory emotions? That's not instability - that's emotional sophistication.

You know what really gets me excited? Seeing people start to grasp this. It's like watching someone discover they've had a superpower all along, they just needed to learn how to access it. Because here's the truth - building emotional stability isn't about becoming some zen master who never gets rattled. It's about developing a relationship with our emotional world that's flexible, resilient, and fundamentally compassionate.

And speaking of compassion - let's talk about how self-compassion plays into all this. Because if there's one thing that can sabotage our stability faster than anything else, it's being harsh with ourselves when we're struggling. It's like trying to learn to dance while someone's constantly stepping on your toes - it just doesn't work.

I'm seeing our time for this section winding down, but I want to emphasize something really important here. Building emotional stability isn't about reaching some mythical endpoint where we're always perfectly regulated. It's about creating a foundation that can weather storms, bounce back from setbacks, and yes, even grow stronger through challenges. It's about developing a relationship with our emotional world that feels like home - sometimes messy, sometimes peaceful, but always, always ours.

You know what really brings this home? The stories I hear from our community. Just last week, I got this incredibly moving email from Sarah - and she gave me permission to share this with you all. She wrote about how she used to think emotional stability meant never feeling anything ""too much."" Like somehow, she was supposed to be this zen master floating above it all.

But here's what changed for her - and this is where it gets really good. She started treating her emotions like weather patterns instead of personal failures. She wrote, ""Gregory, it hit me one day while I was actually watching a storm. The trees that survive aren't the ones that never move - they're the ones that know how to bend.""

That's some profound shit right there, isn't it? And it reminds me of another listener, Marcus, who shared his story during one of our workshop calls. He described how he used to white-knuckle his way through every emotional challenge, thinking that control meant suppression. But after working on his recovery for about two years, he had this breakthrough moment in, of all places, the grocery store.

He was there when someone's kid had a complete meltdown - you know the kind, full nuclear tantrum. And instead of getting triggered like he usually would, he found himself feeling compassion for both the child and the parent. But here's the really interesting part - he noticed his own body tensing up, acknowledged it, and consciously chose to relax his shoulders. He didn't try to force the tension away; he just noticed it, made space for it, and then made a choice about how to respond.

I'm sharing these stories because they illustrate something crucial about building emotional stability - it's not about becoming some emotionless robot or achieving perfect peace 24/7. It's about developing a relationship with our emotional world that's both honest and workable.

Then there's Jamie's story, which I think will resonate with many of you. Jamie had this habit of what they called ""emotional stockpiling"" - pushing down every feeling until they inevitably exploded. Sound familiar to anyone? They described it like trying to hold a beach ball underwater - the harder you push it down, the more forcefully it shoots up when you lose grip.

But here's where Jamie's story gets interesting. They started using this practice of what they called ""emotional check-ins"" - like having a daily coffee date with their feelings. At first, it felt awkward as hell (their words, not mine). But gradually, it became this powerful tool for preventing those massive emotional buildups.

One day, their boss made a passive-aggressive comment in a meeting - the kind of thing that would've previously sent them spiraling. Instead, Jamie noticed the initial sting, acknowledged it, and rather than immediately reacting or suppressing it, they gave themselves permission to process it later during their check-in time. That's emotional stability in action - not the absence of the feeling, but the presence of choice in how we handle it.

I've got to share Lisa's story too, because it shows another crucial aspect of this work. Lisa grew up in what she calls a ""feelings are weakness"" household. Every emotion except happiness was treated like a character flaw. So naturally, she became an expert at emotional suppression, wearing her ""I'm fine"" mask like armor.

The turning point came when her own daughter started showing signs of anxiety. Lisa realized she was unconsciously passing down the same messages she'd received. Talk about a wake-up call, right? But here's where her story gets beautiful - she decided to learn emotional regulation alongside her daughter. They started this practice of naming feelings together, using this silly game where they'd act out different emotions and guess what each other was feeling.

What started as a way to help her daughter became Lisa's own path to emotional stability. She wrote to me, ""I realized I couldn't teach my daughter what I didn't know myself. Now, we have this shared language for feelings, and sometimes she's the one reminding me to check in with myself.""

And you know what? That's exactly how generational healing works. It's messy and imperfect and sometimes we learn our best lessons from the very people we're trying to teach.

Oh, and I can't forget Alex's story - this one still gives me goosebumps. Alex had this brilliant insight about how building emotional stability is like learning to swim. At first, you're terrified of the water (emotions), then you learn to float (basic regulation), and eventually, you can navigate different strokes for different situations. But even Olympic swimmers can still get caught in a riptide - the difference is they know how to work with the water instead of fighting against it.

Alex wrote about how they used to see their emotional sensitivity as a curse, especially in their high-pressure job in emergency services. But through their recovery work, they've come to see it as their superpower. Their ability to feel deeply means they can connect authentically with people in crisis, and their practice of emotional stability means they can do so without getting swept away themselves.

These stories - Sarah's weather patterns, Marcus's grocery store epiphany, Jamie's emotional check-ins, Lisa's generational healing, Alex's swimming metaphor - they all point to the same truth: emotional stability isn't about controlling our feelings; it's about creating a sustainable relationship with them.

And you know what strikes me about all these stories? None of these folks reached some magical endpoint where they never struggle. Instead, they've developed something far more valuable - the confidence that they can handle whatever emotions arise, the wisdom to know that all feelings are temporary, and the compassion to be gentle with themselves through the process.

That's what real emotional stability looks like in recovery. It's not about never falling down; it's about knowing you can get back up, and maybe even learning something valuable in the process. And speaking of learning valuable things, let me share some specific tools and techniques that our community has found helpful in building this kind of stability...

So let's dig into some concrete tools that you can start using today. I want to share what I like to call the ""Stability Toolkit"" - and yeah, I know that sounds a bit clinical, but stick with me here because this stuff actually works.

First up is something I personally use every single day: the emotional weather report. Now, I'm not talking about checking your phone to see if it's gonna rain. This is about taking a moment, maybe when you first wake up or during your morning coffee, to simply notice what's happening in your emotional atmosphere. Are you feeling stormy? Sunny? Maybe there's some emotional fog rolling in? The beauty of this practice is that it helps you separate yourself from the weather - just like real weather, emotions are temporary conditions, not permanent states.

Here's what's really cool about this tool - and I learned this the hard way, trust me - you don't have to do anything about what you notice. The simple act of observing without judgment starts building that stability we're talking about. It's like having a weather station for your inner world.

Now, let me share something that might sound counterintuitive at first. One of the most powerful stability-building tools is actually planning for instability. I know, I know - it sounds weird, right? But hear me out. When I was first working on my own recovery, I used to beat myself up every time I had a bad day or got triggered. Then my therapist said something that changed everything: ""Gregory, what if we expected these moments and had a plan for them?""

This led to what I now call the ""Wobble Protocol"" - and yes, I totally made up that name because it makes me smile, and sometimes we need that in recovery. The idea is to create a simple, clear plan for those moments when you feel yourself starting to lose your emotional footing. Think of it like those emergency procedures on airplanes - you hope you never need them, but damn, it's good to know they're there.

Here's how it works in practice: You identify your early warning signs - maybe it's tension in your shoulders, a racing mind, or that familiar knot in your stomach. Then you create a three-step response plan. Mine looks something like this: First, I acknowledge what's happening - ""Okay, I'm wobbling."" Second, I use a grounding technique - usually putting my hand on my heart and taking three slow breaths. Third, I reach out to someone from my support network or use one of my pre-planned self-care activities.

Speaking of self-care, let's talk about what I call ""Non-Negotiable Nurturing."" This isn't about bubble baths or fancy spa days - though hey, if that's your thing, go for it. This is about identifying the absolute baseline activities that help you maintain stability. For me, it's getting enough sleep, moving my body in some way every day, and having at least one genuine connection with another human being, even if it's just a five-minute phone call.

The key here - and this is crucial - is to make these activities non-negotiable. Treat them like you would taking necessary medication. Because in a very real way, that's what they are - medicine for your nervous system.

Now, here's something that's been incredibly helpful for many folks in our community: the ""Stability Anchor Log."" This is where you keep track of moments when you felt stable, grounded, or managed to recover from a wobble successfully. The brain has this frustrating tendency to forget our wins and remember our struggles, so we need to actively counter that.

I remember one listener - let's call her Sarah - who started keeping her log on her phone. She'd just write quick notes: ""Made it through a difficult meeting without dissociating"" or ""Recognized I was triggered and used my breathing technique."" After a few weeks, she had this amazing resource of personal evidence that she could handle more than she thought.

Let me also share a tool that's particularly useful for those times when everything feels like too much: the ""Five Senses Check-In."" This is a variation on the classic grounding technique, but with a twist. Instead of just naming things you can see, hear, etc., you use your senses to find something that brings you a tiny bit of comfort or pleasure. Maybe it's the warmth of sunlight on your face, the smell of fresh coffee, or the texture of your favorite sweater.

The power of this tool lies in its simplicity and accessibility. You can do it anywhere, anytime, and no one even needs to know you're doing it. It's like having an emotional reset button in your pocket.

Remember, building emotional stability isn't about never experiencing difficult emotions or never getting triggered. It's about creating a foundation solid enough to weather the storms when they come. These tools are like the pillars of that foundation - the more you use them, the stronger they become.

And hey, if some of these tools don't resonate with you, that's completely okay. Recovery isn't one-size-fits-all. The best approach is to try different things, keep what works for you, and modify what doesn't. Think of it as creating your own personal stability recipe - one ingredient at a time.

Let's move on to explore some additional strategies that can help you maintain this stability once you start building it...

So let's dig into some additional tools that can really help cement this stability we're working on. You know, one thing I've learned both personally and from working with countless survivors is that stability isn't just about the big dramatic moments - it's about creating these small, reliable anchors throughout your day.

Here's something that's been incredibly helpful for me: setting up what I call ""stability checkpoints."" Think of them like those save points in video games - places where you can pause and recalibrate. Maybe it's when you first wake up, during your lunch break, or right before bed. The key is to make these check-ins brief but meaningful. Take thirty seconds to notice your breathing, scan your body for tension, and just get a read on where you're at emotionally.

Now, I know what some of you might be thinking - ""Greg, I can barely remember to eat lunch, let alone do emotional check-ins."" Trust me, I get it. That's why I suggest piggybacking these moments onto things you already do routinely. Like, every time you wash your hands, take three deep breaths. Or whenever you get in your car, spend the first few seconds grounding yourself before turning on the engine.

Let me tell you about something that happened with one of my clients - we'll call her Sarah. She was struggling with emotional flooding at work, those moments when everything becomes too much and you feel like you're drowning in overwhelm. We developed what she called her ""desk anchor"" - a small smooth stone she kept next to her computer. Whenever she felt those familiar waves of anxiety rising, she'd hold the stone, focus on its texture, and use it as a physical reminder to return to her breath and body.

This brings me to another powerful tool: creating environmental stability. Your physical space has a huge impact on your emotional state. Now, I'm not saying you need to go full Marie Kondo here, but having certain areas in your life that remain consistent and controlled can provide a crucial foundation when everything else feels chaotic.

For instance, I have this one corner in my living room - just a comfortable chair, a small table, and a lamp. It's my designated ""stability zone."" When things get overwhelming, I know that space is always there, unchanged, waiting for me. It's like having an emotional home base, if you will.

Something else that's been transformative for many survivors is what I call the ""future self check-in."" When you're making decisions, especially in moments of stress or uncertainty, ask yourself: ""What would my stable future self want me to do right now?"" It's a way of building trust with yourself and creating continuity between your present and future emotional states.

Let's talk about relationships for a minute, because they're crucial for emotional stability. Many of us with CPTSD have learned to either isolate ourselves or become overly dependent on others. The sweet spot is building what I call a ""stability network"" - a carefully curated group of people who understand and support your recovery journey. This isn't about having a huge social circle; it's about having reliable connections you can count on.

Here's a practical tip for building that network: start with one person. Just one. Be honest with them about what you're working on and what kind of support you need. Maybe it's someone who's okay with you sending a quick text when you're feeling wobbly, or someone who can remind you of your progress when you're stuck in a shame spiral.

Another tool that's been incredibly helpful is creating what I call ""stability scripts"" - short, personalized phrases that help you navigate triggering situations. These aren't generic affirmations; they're specific to your experiences and needs. For example, instead of ""I am safe,"" which might feel false in the moment, try something like ""I have tools to handle this feeling"" or ""This intensity will pass.""

Remember, building emotional stability is like developing any other skill - it takes practice, patience, and persistence. You're not going to nail it perfectly every time, and that's completely okay. In fact, those moments when things don't go as planned are valuable opportunities to strengthen your stability muscles.

The goal isn't to never feel destabilized - that's not realistic or even desirable. The goal is to build a strong enough foundation that when the waves come (and they will come), you have multiple tools and strategies to help you stay afloat and find your way back to center.

You know what's wild about this whole emotional stability journey? It's a lot like learning to surf. At first, you're just trying not to drown - and that's completely normal. But gradually, you start to recognize the patterns in the waves, you build those stability muscles we talked about, and one day you realize you're actually riding those waves instead of being crushed by them.

Look, I know some days it feels like you're right back at square one. Trust me, I've been there - we all have. But here's what I want you to remember: every single tool we discussed today, every strategy you try, every time you pause to check in with yourself - that's you building your emotional surfboard. That's you getting stronger.

And the beautiful thing is, you don't have to do this perfectly. Some days you'll nail it, catching those emotional waves like a pro. Other days, you'll wipe out spectacularly - and that's okay too. Because now you know how to get back up, how to recenter, how to reach out when you need support.

Remember, this isn't about becoming some zen master who never feels anything. It's about building a relationship with your emotions that feels manageable, authentic, and ultimately empowering. You're not broken - you're learning. And you're doing it brilliantly, one small step at a time.

Next week, we're going to dive into something really important - celebrating those small wins. Because trust me, they matter more than you think.

The ones we usually brush off as ""just doing the bare minimum"" or ""nothing special."" Well, guess what? They are special, and that's exactly what we're unpacking today.

For those of us with Complex PTSD, our brains have been wired to focus on threat, danger, and what could go wrong. It's like having an overactive smoke detector that goes off when someone's just making toast three blocks away. This hypervigilance served us well during trauma, but now? It's making us miss the beautiful little victories that pepper our days.

Throughout this episode, we're going to explore why celebrating small wins isn't just feel-good fluff - it's actually crucial for rewiring our trauma-affected brains. I'll share some stories from our community (with permission, of course) that'll probably sound familiar to many of you. We'll look at the science behind why acknowledging our progress, no matter how tiny it seems, can create lasting positive changes in our recovery journey.

You'll learn practical ways to start recognizing and celebrating these wins without feeling silly or self-indulgent. And for those of you thinking, ""But Gregory, I don't have any wins to celebrate"" - stick around. I promise you'll start seeing them everywhere once you know what to look for.

So grab your favorite comfort item, settle in somewhere safe, and let's explore how these small celebrations can become powerful stepping stones on your healing path. Remember, you're not just surviving anymore - you're learning to thrive, one tiny victory at a time.

Now, before we dive deeper into celebrating those wins, let's take a moment to check in with our bodies. I know, I know - some of you might be rolling your eyes thinking ""Here goes Gregory with the woo-woo stuff again."" But trust me on this one, friends. Our bodies hold so much wisdom, and they're usually trying to tell us something important.

Take a nice deep breath with me. Not one of those shallow chest breaths we do when we're stressed - I'm talking about a real belly breath. Feel your belly expand like a balloon as you inhale... and then deflate as you exhale. No judgment here if your breath feels a bit shaky or if you're having trouble connecting with it. That's totally normal when we're dealing with CPTSD.

Notice where you are right now. Maybe you're curled up on your couch, or perhaps you're driving (keep those eyes on the road!), or maybe you're hiding in your bathroom for a moment of peace - wherever you are, that's perfect. Just notice the points where your body makes contact with whatever's supporting you.

Here's something interesting I've noticed in my own journey - when we start acknowledging our small wins, our bodies often respond before our minds do. You might feel a subtle release in your shoulders, a warmth in your chest, or maybe even a flutter of excitement in your belly. These physical sensations are like little high-fives from your nervous system saying, ""Hey, we're doing okay!""

Take another breath with me. As you exhale, see if you can let go of any tension you're holding - even just a tiny bit. Your jaw, your shoulders, your hands... wherever you notice tightness, send a little breath there. And if you can't relax right now, that's okay too. Sometimes being aware of the tension is a win in itself.

I want you to know that whatever you're feeling in your body right now - whether it's anxiety, exhaustion, numbness, or maybe even a spark of hope - it's all valid. Your body has carried you through so much, and it's still here, still fighting, still showing up. That itself is worth celebrating.

As we move forward in our discussion about celebrating small wins, try to stay connected to these physical sensations. Your body might have its own way of celebrating - maybe a spontaneous smile, a deeper breath, or even just a moment where your shoulders drop away from your ears. These are all tiny victories, my friends, and they matter more than you might think.

Let's take one more breath together before we continue. You're doing great, even if it doesn't feel like it right now.

You know what's fascinating? When I first started my healing journey, I used to get so frustrated with myself for not making ""enough"" progress. I'd look at these massive goals I'd set - like ""never dissociate again"" or ""be completely healed by next month"" - and beat myself up when I inevitably fell short. Talk about setting yourself up for disappointment, right?

But here's where it gets interesting: our brains literally reshape themselves through small, consistent changes. Not the grand, sweeping transformations we sometimes dream about. It's kind of like trying to redirect a river - you don't do it all at once. You start with tiny channels, little by little, until eventually, the water finds a new path.

Let me share something that happened just last week. I was at the grocery store - you know, just doing that regular mundane life stuff - and I started feeling that familiar tension creeping up. In the past, I would've either pushed through until I was completely overwhelmed or just abandoned my cart and bolted. But this time, I noticed it. I actually noticed it before it got too intense. And you know what I did? I took a thirty-second break in the cereal aisle. Just stood there, breathing, while pretending to be really interested in fiber content.

That might not sound like much to someone who hasn't lived with CPTSD, but holy shit, friends - that was a win. That was me showing up for myself in a way that my younger self never experienced. And that's exactly the kind of small victory we need to acknowledge.

Think about it this way: If you were teaching a kid to ride a bike, you wouldn't wait until they could do a perfect Tour de France stage to celebrate, right? You'd celebrate when they balanced for three seconds, when they made it to the end of the driveway, when they got back up after falling. Our healing journey deserves that same kind of gentle recognition.

Here's what I've learned: these small wins compound. That moment in the grocery store? It built on hundreds of tiny moments before it - times when I practiced grounding techniques in less stressful situations, times when I simply noticed my triggers without judging them, even times when I messed up but showed myself compassion afterward.

And speaking of compassion - let's talk about how we often dismiss our progress because it doesn't look like someone else's healing journey. That's like comparing your chapter 3 to someone else's chapter 20. Your wins are valid precisely because they're yours. They're calibrated to your specific challenges, your unique history, your personal battle with CPTSD.

Sometimes a win might look like sending that email you've been avoiding for weeks. Sometimes it's setting a boundary, even if your voice shakes while doing it. Sometimes it's just managing to get out of bed when your body feels like it's made of lead. These aren't small things - they're evidence of you actively rewiring neural pathways that were shaped by trauma.

The beauty of celebrating these moments isn't just about feeling good - though that's important too. It's about creating new evidence for your nervous system that change is possible, that you're capable of growth, that you're worthy of acknowledgment. Each celebration becomes a tiny but powerful vote for your recovery, a small but significant step toward believing in your own resilience.

So let's put this into practice right now with a simple but powerful exercise I like to call the ""Win Window."" I want you to grab something to write with - don't worry, I'll wait a few seconds while you do that. Got it? Perfect.

Now, here's what we're going to do together. We're going to create a small window of time - just three minutes - where you focus entirely on identifying your wins from the past 24 hours. And I mean everything, my friends. Did you brush your teeth this morning? That's a win. Did you take a deep breath when you felt overwhelmed instead of spiraling? Absolutely a win. Maybe you sent that text you've been avoiding, or simply got out of bed even though your body felt like lead.

I'm going to be quiet for three minutes while you write these down, but before we start, I want to address something I know is happening in some of your minds right now. You might be thinking, ""Gregory, this is stupid. Getting out of bed isn't an achievement."" Or maybe, ""I can't think of anything good I've done."" I hear you. That inner critic is loud, I know. But remember what we talked about earlier - this isn't about meeting some external standard of success. This is about recognizing the courage it takes to keep moving forward when you're carrying the weight of trauma.

Let's do this together. I'm setting my timer for three minutes. Write down every single thing you've done in the last 24 hours that took any amount of effort, any choice you made that supported your wellbeing, any moment you showed up for yourself or others. No win is too small. Hell, write down that you're listening to this podcast - that's you actively engaging in your healing journey!

Okay, time's up. Now, here's the crucial part - and this is where most of us struggle - I want you to look at each item you wrote down and say, out loud if you can, ""I acknowledge this win."" Not ""but it should have been better"" or ""anyone could do this."" Just pure acknowledgment. Feel free to pause the podcast if you need more time with this part.

You know what's beautiful about this practice? It starts training your brain to notice these moments in real-time. It's like installing a new program that runs in the background, catching these small victories as they happen instead of letting them slip by unnoticed. And each time you acknowledge these wins, you're literally rewiring those neural pathways that trauma has distorted.

Try to do this Win Window exercise daily for the next week. You might feel resistant or silly at first - that's totally normal. But stick with it. You're not just collecting wins; you're building a new relationship with yourself, one tiny celebration at a time.

You know what else has been really powerful for me and so many folks I've worked with? Something I like to call the ""Anti-Shame Share."" Now, this might sound a bit strange at first, but stay with me here.

Here's how it works: Pick someone you trust - could be a friend, therapist, partner, or even a supportive online community. When you notice yourself achieving something that your trauma brain wants to dismiss, share it with them. But here's the key - you have to share it proudly, like you're bragging a little bit. Yeah, I know, that probably made some of you cringe just now. That's exactly why we need to do it.

Let me give you an example from my own life. Last week, I managed to get out of bed before noon three days in a row. Now, for some people, that might sound ridiculously basic. But for those of us who've dealt with CPTSD-related depression, we know what a fucking victory that can be. So I called my sister and actually said, ""Hey, guess what? I'm crushing it this week with my morning routine!"" Did it feel weird? Hell yes. Did I want to immediately follow it with ten reasons why it wasn't actually impressive? Also yes. But I didn't.

The magic happens when we push through that discomfort. Every time we deliberately celebrate something our shame wants to minimize, we're basically giving trauma the middle finger. We're rewiring those old patterns that taught us to hide our light.

What I love about this practice is how it creates this beautiful ripple effect. When you share your wins with someone else, you're not just healing yourself - you're giving them permission to celebrate their own wins too. It's like this gorgeous rebellion against shame, one tiny celebration at a time.

Start small. Maybe text a friend about making your bed or cooking a meal. Share in our online community about setting a boundary, even if it wasn't perfect. The size of the win doesn't matter - what matters is the act of claiming it, owning it, and saying ""Yeah, I did that.""

Remember, this isn't about bragging rights or comparing yourself to others. It's about reclaiming your right to feel good about your progress, to take up space with your joy, to let your wins be wins without qualification or apology. Because here's the truth - every time you celebrate openly, you're not just changing your own narrative; you're helping create a world where it's safer for all of us to acknowledge our growth, however small it might seem.

You know what I find fascinating? The way these small celebrations actually rewire our nervous systems over time. It's like we're creating new neural pathways - superhighways of self-compassion, if you will. And yeah, I know that sounds a bit woo-woo, but stick with me here.

Think about how many times you've dismissed your progress because it didn't feel ""big enough."" I did that for years. Hell, I still catch myself doing it sometimes. But here's what changed everything for me: I started keeping what I call my ""Tiny Victories Journal."" Nothing fancy - just quick notes about moments when I chose differently than my trauma responses would have dictated.

The first entry? ""Took a deep breath instead of immediately apologizing when someone bumped into ME at the grocery store."" Seems ridiculous now, but that moment? That was huge for my recovery journey.

So here's what I want you to consider doing this week: Pick one small win - just one - and really let yourself feel good about it. Maybe you set a boundary with your phone by not checking work emails after 7 PM. Maybe you allowed yourself to rest without feeling guilty. Whatever it is, give yourself permission to celebrate it fully.

Because here's the real magic - when we acknowledge these small wins, we're not just marking progress. We're actively participating in our own healing. We're telling our nervous systems, ""Hey, we're safe enough now to celebrate. We're safe enough to grow.""

And if you're sitting there thinking, ""But Gregory, I don't know if I can do this..."" - that's okay too. Start wherever you are. Even the thought ""Maybe I could try celebrating small wins"" is itself a win worth celebrating.
