I can already hear some of you sighing, thinking ""Great, another person telling me to take bubble baths and do yoga."" But hang with me here, because that's not what we're talking about today. When you're dealing with CPTSD, traditional self-care advice often feels about as helpful as a chocolate teapot. We need something real, something that understands the unique challenges we face.

In today's episode, we're going to dig deep into creating a self-care plan that actually makes sense for people with CPTSD. And I mean really makes sense - not just some generic checklist that looks pretty on Pinterest but falls apart the moment you're triggered or experiencing an emotional flashback.

I'm going to walk you through what a CPTSD-specific self-care plan looks like, and we'll explore why the usual approaches often don't cut it for us. We'll talk about how to build a plan that takes into account your window of tolerance, your triggers, and your specific needs during different phases of recovery.

You'll hear some real stories from our community members who've figured out what works for them - some approaches might surprise you, and that's okay because we're all different. We'll also dive into some research-backed strategies that you can adapt to your own situation, and I'll share some practical tools that have helped both me and many of my clients.

Look, I know some days just getting out of bed feels like climbing Mount Everest. That's why we're going to focus on creating a plan that's flexible enough to work on your worst days and your best days. We'll talk about how to identify what you actually need - not what you think you should need - and how to build a sustainable practice that doesn't feel like another item on your to-do list.

By the end of this episode, you'll have a clear framework for developing your own personalized self-care plan, one that acknowledges the reality of living with CPTSD while giving you practical ways to support yourself through the healing process.

So grab whatever makes you comfortable - your favorite beverage, a cozy blanket, or maybe your trusted pet - and let's dive into making sense of self-care in a way that actually works for us.

You know, when I first started my healing journey, I thought self-care meant bubble baths and scented candles. Don't get me wrong - if those work for you, that's fantastic. But for those of us with CPTSD, we often need something a bit more... substantial. Let me tell you about the time I had what I now call my ""self-care wake-up call.""

I was sitting in my therapist's office, completely burned out, barely holding it together. She asked me what I was doing to take care of myself, and I proudly listed off all my ""self-care"" activities - meditation apps I never opened, yoga classes I kept meaning to attend, and journals I'd bought but never written in. She just looked at me with that knowing therapist smile and said, ""Greg, that's not self-care. That's a wish list.""

And damn, did that hit home. Here's the thing about CPTSD - it fundamentally changes how we relate to ourselves and our needs. Many of us grew up in environments where our needs were either ignored or used against us. So when everyone starts throwing around terms like ""self-care,"" we either dismiss it as selfish or turn it into another way to beat ourselves up for not doing enough.

Let me be really clear here - creating a self-care plan for CPTSD isn't about adding more items to your to-do list. It's about building a sustainable framework that helps you navigate life with this condition. Think of it like creating your own personal operations manual - except this time, you get to write it yourself, based on what actually works for you.

I remember when I first started developing my own self-care plan. It was messy, folks. Really messy. I had days where my idea of self-care was just managing to brush my teeth, and you know what? That was perfectly valid. Because here's what I've learned - self-care with CPTSD isn't about achieving some Instagram-worthy lifestyle. It's about meeting yourself where you are and building from there.

The tricky part about CPTSD is that it can make even basic self-care feel impossible. Our nervous systems are often so dysregulated that the mere thought of ""taking care of ourselves"" can trigger anxiety or shame. I've been there - that place where making dinner feels as overwhelming as climbing Mount Everest, where taking a shower seems like an insurmountable task.

But here's what changed everything for me: understanding that self-care isn't just about the actions we take - it's about the relationship we're building with ourselves. It's about learning to listen to our needs without judgment, about creating safety in our own bodies and minds. And let me tell you, that's a whole different ballgame from what most people think of as self-care.

I had this moment last year - I was having a particularly rough day, feeling triggered and overwhelmed. My old pattern would have been to push through, ignore the signs, and eventually crash. But instead, I remembered what we're going to talk about today. I stopped, checked in with myself, and realized what I needed wasn't on any conventional self-care checklist. What I needed was to sit in my car for 20 minutes, listening to terrible 90s pop music and letting myself feel whatever came up.

That's the kind of self-care we're going to explore today - the real, messy, personalized kind that actually makes a difference when you're dealing with CPTSD. We're going to look at how to create a self-care plan that doesn't just look good on paper but actually supports you through the tough stuff.

And listen, I know some of you might be thinking, ""Great, another thing I need to figure out how to do."" I get it. The last thing any of us needs is more pressure. But that's exactly why we need to talk about this - because when done right, a self-care plan isn't another burden. It's a lifeline, a framework that helps us navigate the challenges of living with CPTSD.

So in this episode, we're going to break it down into manageable pieces. We'll look at what self-care really means in the context of CPTSD, how to identify what you actually need (not what Instagram tells you you need), and how to create a plan that's flexible enough to adapt to your changing needs. Because if there's one thing I know about CPTSD, it's that our needs can change from moment to moment, and our self-care plan needs to be able to roll with those changes.

Let's get real about what it takes to create a self-care practice that actually serves us, rather than just adding to our stress. Because you deserve support that actually works for you, not just what works on paper.

So here's what I've learned after years of both struggling with and supporting others through CPTSD - creating a self-care plan isn't about making some pristine schedule that looks great on Instagram. It's about getting real with where you're at right now and building from there. And I mean really real - like ""sometimes I can barely get out of bed"" real.

Let me tell you about what happened when I first tried to create my own self-care plan. I did what probably many of you have done - I made this elaborate plan with morning meditation, yoga, journaling, healthy meals, and all that good stuff. You know what happened? I lasted exactly two and a half days before completely abandoning it because it was just too much. And then I felt like shit about myself because clearly I was ""failing"" at self-care too.

Here's the thing about CPTSD - it already comes with enough self-judgment and impossible standards. We don't need to add more pressure with unrealistic self-care expectations. What we need instead is what I like to call ""trauma-informed self-care."" This means creating a plan that actually accounts for our triggers, our energy levels, and those times when we're deep in an emotional flashback.

Now, this might sound obvious, but it took me years to figure out that self-care isn't just about the good days. It's actually most important for the bad days. Those days when your inner critic is screaming, your body feels like lead, and everything seems overwhelming - that's when you need your self-care plan the most. But here's the catch - it needs to be simple enough that you can actually do it when you're in that state.

I remember one of my clients - let's call her Sarah - who had this breakthrough moment about this. She was beating herself up because she couldn't stick to her ""wellness routine"" during her hard days. We worked together to create what she called her ""bare minimum plan"" - the absolute basics she could manage even on her worst days. Sometimes it was just drinking water and taking her medications. And you know what? That was enough. That was self-care too.

The real magic happens when we start building our self-care plan like a set of Russian nesting dolls. Stay with me here - I promise this makes sense. Your smallest doll is your crisis plan - the absolute basics you need when you're at your lowest. Maybe it's just breathing exercises and calling your therapist. The next size up might be your ""rough day"" plan - slightly more involved but still manageable. Then you have your ""okay day"" plan, and finally, your ""good day"" plan when you have more energy and capacity.

Let's get practical about this for a minute. When you're creating your plan, start with the smallest doll first. Ask yourself: ""What do I absolutely need to function?"" We're talking bare basics here - hygiene, medication, food, water, rest. Then, and only then, do you start building out the other layers.

Something that's really important to understand is that self-care isn't just about doing nice things for yourself - though that's definitely part of it. It's about creating safety for your nervous system. This means different things for different people. For some, it might be having a strict morning routine because predictability feels safe. For others, it might be having lots of flexibility because rigid schedules trigger anxiety.

I've noticed in my own journey that some of my most effective self-care practices aren't the ones you'd typically see on a wellness blog. Sometimes it's setting boundaries with people who drain my energy. Sometimes it's allowing myself to stay in bed for an extra hour when I'm overwhelmed. Sometimes it's ordering takeout instead of forcing myself to cook when I'm exhausted.

Here's a truth bomb for you: Self-care isn't always going to feel good in the moment. Sometimes it's about doing the hard thing because you know it's what you need. Like reaching out to your support system when shame is telling you to isolate. Or going to therapy even when you'd rather avoid dealing with your stuff. That's self-care too.

The key is to make your plan flexible enough to adapt to your changing needs, but structured enough to provide some sense of stability. Think of it like a choose-your-own-adventure book rather than a rigid schedule. You want options for different scenarios, different energy levels, different emotional states.

And remember, your self-care plan isn't set in stone. It's going to evolve as you do. What works for you today might not work next month, and that's okay. The goal isn't perfection - it's progress. It's about building a relationship with yourself where you learn to recognize and respond to your own needs with compassion and wisdom.

This might sound like a lot, and it kind of is. But we're going to break it down into manageable pieces. Because that's what this is all about - taking this overwhelming thing called CPTSD recovery and making it something we can actually work with, one small step at a time.

So let's dig into what the research actually tells us about self-care plans for CPTSD recovery, because - and I know this will shock you - there's some pretty fascinating science behind why this stuff actually works. And spoiler alert: it's not just about bubble baths and scented candles, though hey, if that's your jam, I'm not judging.

A groundbreaking study from van der Kolk's trauma center - and if you're new here, Bessel van der Kolk is basically the godfather of trauma research - showed that structured self-care practices significantly reduced PTSD symptoms in 76% of participants. But here's the kicker: it wasn't just any kind of self-care. The most effective plans incorporated what researchers call ""bottom-up"" and ""top-down"" approaches.

Now, I know that sounds like some kind of sandwich-making technique, but stick with me here. Bottom-up approaches are things that start with the body - movement, breathing, sensory experiences. Top-down approaches involve our thinking brain - meditation, journaling, cognitive work. And when we combine both? That's where the magic happens.

Recent research from the International Journal of Trauma Studies shows that people with CPTSD who implemented structured self-care plans were three times more likely to maintain long-term recovery progress compared to those who approached recovery more randomly. And look, I'm not just throwing these numbers at you to sound smart - this matters because it tells us something crucial: we're not just making this shit up.

There's this fascinating study from 2021 that particularly caught my attention - it looked at what they called ""recovery resilience factors."" They found that people who included all five key areas in their self-care plans - physical, emotional, social, spiritual (whatever that means to you), and practical life management - showed significantly better outcomes than those who focused on just one or two areas. And when I say significantly better, I mean like 65% better reduction in flashbacks and emotional dysregulation.

But here's something that made me laugh - in the best possible way. The researchers also found that perfection was actually the enemy of progress. Participants who aimed for ""perfect"" execution of their self-care plans actually had worse outcomes than those who approached it with what they called ""flexible consistency."" In other words, doing something imperfectly but regularly beat the hell out of doing it perfectly but sporadically.

Now, let's talk about the neuroscience for a minute, because this is where it gets really interesting. Dr. Stephen Porges - and if you've been with me a while, you know I'm a huge Polyvagal Theory nerd - his research shows that consistent self-care practices actually help regulate our nervous system over time. It's like we're literally rewiring our threat detection system. And that's not just fancy talk - they can actually see these changes on brain scans.

A 2022 study published in the Journal of Traumatic Stress showed something fascinating about timing. They found that small, frequent self-care activities throughout the day had a more positive impact than longer, less frequent sessions. It's like giving your nervous system little sips of safety throughout the day instead of trying to chug a whole bottle of calm all at once.

But here's what I think is really important - and this comes from a meta-analysis of over 50 studies on trauma recovery - there's no one-size-fits-all approach. What works amazingly for one person might be completely unhelpful for another. And that's actually backed by science! The research shows that the most successful self-care plans are the ones that are personalized to the individual's specific needs, triggers, and resources.

Here's something else that blew my mind: A study from the Complex Trauma Research Network found that people who included what they called ""micro-practices"" - tiny self-care actions that take less than two minutes - were more likely to stick with their overall plan. We're talking super simple stuff like taking three deep breaths before checking your phone in the morning, or doing a quick body scan while waiting for your coffee to brew.

And let me tell you about this really cool longitudinal study that tracked CPTSD recovery over five years. They found that people who regularly reviewed and adjusted their self-care plans - like, every few months - had better outcomes than those who stuck rigidly to the same plan. It's like our recovery needs evolve, and our self-care needs to evolve with it.

But perhaps the most validating piece of research I've come across - and this is from a 2023 study - shows that even small improvements in self-care consistency led to measurable improvements in quality of life. We're not talking about massive transformations here - even a 10% increase in consistent self-care practices was associated with significant reduction in CPTSD symptoms.

So what does all this research tell us? It tells us that we're not just throwing spaghetti at the wall here. The science backs up what many of us have learned the hard way - that structured, personalized self-care isn't just a nice-to-have, it's a fundamental part of CPTSD recovery. And most importantly, it tells us that we don't have to do it perfectly - we just have to do it consistently, in a way that works for us.

Now, here's something that took me way too long to figure out - your self-care plan needs to evolve as you do. I used to beat myself up when strategies that worked great for months suddenly felt... off. Like that meditation app that was my lifeline last year? Yeah, started feeling more like a chore than a refuge. And you know what? That's completely normal.

Let me tell you about what I call the ""self-care seasons."" Just like we don't wear a winter coat in summer, our CPTSD recovery needs different types of care at different times. Some days, self-care might look like getting out of bed and managing to eat something - and that's totally valid. Other days, it might mean tackling that art project you've been dreaming about or finally setting a boundary with that energy-draining friend.

Speaking of boundaries - holy shit, did I struggle with this one. For the longest time, I thought self-care was selfish, like I was somehow stealing time from more ""important"" things. But here's the mind-bending truth I finally got through my thick skull: setting boundaries IS self-care. When we say ""no"" to things that drain us, we're saying ""yes"" to our own healing.

So, let's talk about something that rarely gets mentioned in those glossy wellness magazines - crisis planning as part of self-care. Because let's be real, triggers and emotional flashbacks don't politely wait for convenient moments. They show up uninvited, like that relative who always crashes on your couch without warning. Having a solid crisis plan isn't being negative - it's being smart as fuck.

I remember one particularly rough patch where my usual grounding techniques just weren't cutting it. My therapist suggested something that sounded ridiculous at first - creating different levels of self-care plans. Like, having a ""green zone"" plan for good days, ""yellow zone"" for wobbly times, and ""red zone"" for those moments when everything feels like it's falling apart. Turns out, it was brilliant. Because when you're in the middle of an emotional storm, trying to figure out what you need is like trying to read a map in a hurricane.

Here's something else that blew my mind - self-care isn't just about managing the hard stuff. It's also about creating space for joy, even tiny moments of it. And I know, I know, when you're dealing with CPTSD, joy can feel about as accessible as a beach vacation on Mars. But that's exactly why we need to intentionally plan for it.

Think about it this way - trauma rewired our brains to expect danger around every corner. Creating deliberate moments of pleasure, safety, and connection is like being a gentle electrician, rewiring those circuits back to something healthier. Maybe it's five minutes with your face in the sun, or dancing badly to your favorite song, or petting that neighborhood cat that always seems to know when you need some furry therapy.

And can we talk about the guilt? Because holy hell, the guilt about taking care of ourselves can be overwhelming. That voice that says ""You don't deserve this"" or ""You're being self-indulgent"" - yeah, I know that voice well. It's like having an internal critic on steroids. But here's what I've learned: that voice is part of the trauma, not the truth. Every time we choose self-care despite that voice, we're basically giving trauma the middle finger.

Something else that's crucial - your self-care plan needs to include connection with others who get it. And I don't mean those toxic positivity folks who think you can just ""choose happiness"" and magically heal decades of trauma. I mean real, authentic connection with people who understand that recovery isn't linear, who won't judge you for having bad days, who get why sometimes you need to cancel plans because your nervous system is screaming.

Let's be honest - building a self-care practice when you have CPTSD is like trying to renovate a house while you're still living in it. It's messy, sometimes things get worse before they get better, and there are definitely days when it feels like everything's falling apart. But every small act of self-care is like adding another brick to your foundation of healing.

Remember this: your self-care plan doesn't have to look Instagram-worthy. It doesn't have to involve expensive spa treatments or complicated meditation routines (unless that's your thing, in which case, rock on). It just needs to work for you, in your real life, with your real challenges and limitations.

And here's something I wish someone had told me earlier - it's okay if your self-care looks different from everyone else's. Maybe your version of self-care is setting seventeen alarms because CPTSD messes with your sleep. Maybe it's having frozen meals ready for the days when cooking feels impossible. Maybe it's keeping a ""fuck everything"" playlist ready for emergency dance parties in your kitchen. Whatever works for you is valid.

The point isn't to create some perfect, unchanging routine. The point is to build a flexible, personalized toolkit that helps you navigate life with CPTSD. Because at the end of the day, self-care isn't about achieving some idealized version of healing - it's about supporting ourselves through the messy, beautiful, challenging journey of recovery.

You know what really brings this all home? The stories I hear from our community. Like Sarah's email from last week - she wrote about how she used to beat herself up for not having the ""perfect"" self-care routine. She'd see all these Instagram posts about morning meditation and green smoothies, and feel like a complete failure because some days she could barely get out of bed.

But here's what changed everything for her - she started small. Like, really small. She picked just one thing: keeping a water bottle by her bed. That's it. Just making sure she stayed hydrated. And you know what? That tiny action became her anchor. On really rough days, even if everything else felt impossible, she could still reach for that water bottle and think, ""Okay, I'm doing something good for myself.""

I got another message from Marcus that really hit home. He shared how his therapist kept pushing him to do yoga and journaling, and while those are fantastic tools for some folks, they just weren't working for him. He felt worse because these ""standard"" self-care practices weren't helping. Then one day, he realized that working on his car - something he'd loved doing since he was a kid - was actually his best form of self-care. The familiar smell of motor oil, the methodical process of fixing things, the sense of accomplishment - it was his meditation.

And that's exactly what we're talking about here, folks. Self-care isn't one-size-fits-all. Sometimes it looks like what Jen described in her story - she created what she calls her ""emergency toolkit."" It's literally a shoebox filled with things that help ground her during flashbacks: a smooth stone from her favorite beach, lavender-scented hand cream, photos of her dog, and a playlist of songs that make her feel safe. She keeps one at home and a smaller version in her car.

Then there's Alex's story, which I think many of you might relate to. They struggled with the concept of ""deserving"" self-care. Growing up in an environment where their needs were constantly dismissed, the very idea of taking care of themselves felt selfish and wrong. They shared how they started by reframing self-care as ""maintenance"" - like how you wouldn't feel guilty about changing your car's oil or charging your phone. This shift in perspective was huge for them.

I remember getting this powerful message from Rachel about how she had to completely reimagine what self-care meant during a particularly rough patch of depression. She said, ""Some days, self-care meant letting the dishes sit in the sink and ordering takeout. Other days, it meant pushing myself to cook a proper meal because I knew my body needed it. The real self-care was learning to listen to what I actually needed in each moment, without judgment.""

And that's such a crucial point, isn't it? The judgment piece. Maria wrote to me about how she used to have this all-or-nothing approach to self-care. If she couldn't do her full morning routine - exercise, meditation, journaling, healthy breakfast - she'd think, ""Well, I've already failed, so why bother doing anything?"" Now she celebrates the small wins. Maybe she only managed to brush her teeth and take her meds today - that's still self-care, that still matters.

I particularly love what David shared about incorporating his cultural practices into his self-care routine. Growing up in a Latino family, he felt disconnected from his roots after trauma. He found healing in returning to some of his grandmother's rituals - making tea from herbs she used to grow, playing the music she loved, cooking the recipes she taught him. It reminded me how self-care can also be about reconnecting with parts of ourselves we might have lost along the way.

And let's talk about what Katie discovered about timing. She used to try to force herself to do self-care activities first thing in the morning because that's what all the wellness experts recommended. But she's a night owl, and fighting her natural rhythms was just adding more stress. Once she started doing her self-care routine in the evening instead, it actually stuck. She wrote, ""I finally realized that the 'when' matters as much as the 'what.'""

These stories really highlight something important: self-care plans need to evolve as we do. Like Emma shared, what worked for her during the initial stages of processing her trauma looked very different from what she needs now. Early on, she needed lots of alone time and gentle activities. Now, she's gradually incorporating more social connections and challenging experiences into her self-care routine.

And you know what? That's exactly how it should be. Our needs change, our capacity changes, our circumstances change - and our self-care plans should reflect that. As Mike put it in his message, ""I used to think I was failing at self-care because I couldn't stick to one routine. Now I understand that adapting my self-care to match where I'm at is actually a sign of growth, not failure.""

These stories from our community really drive home what we've been talking about. They show us that there's no ""right"" way to do self-care, that it's okay to experiment and adjust, and most importantly, that we're not alone in this journey. Every single person who shared their story started exactly where you might be right now - trying to figure out what works for them, making mistakes, learning, and gradually building their own unique approach to taking care of themselves.

Now, let's roll up our sleeves and get into the nitty-gritty of actually building this self-care plan. I know some of you are probably thinking, ""Greg, just tell me what to do already!"" And I hear you. After all the theory and stories, it's time for the hands-on stuff.

Let's start with something I call the ""Body-Mind Check-In."" It's deceptively simple, but stick with me here. Take a moment right now - yes, literally right now, unless you're driving - and notice three things: your breath, your shoulders, and your jaw. These three areas tend to hold onto our stress like they're collecting frequent flier miles. Just notice them. No judgment, no trying to fix anything. This is your baseline, your starting point.

You know what's wild? Most of us with CPTSD spend so much time in our heads that we've practically sublet our bodies to our trauma. So this check-in becomes your anchor, your way of coming home to yourself. Try doing it three times a day - morning, afternoon, and evening. Set a gentle alarm if you need to. I used to have mine labeled ""Hey, you still have a body!"" because sometimes we need that reminder with a side of humor.

Here's where it gets interesting. After your check-in, rate your overall stress level on a scale from 1-10. Don't overthink it; your first instinct is usually right. Keep track of these numbers for a week. You'll start to notice patterns - maybe your stress spikes after certain activities or interactions. This information is gold for your self-care plan.

Now, let's talk about what I call the ""Emergency Kit."" This isn't your typical first aid kit, though Band-Aids are always handy. This is your personalized collection of comfort items and tools that help ground you when things get rough. Mine includes a smooth stone I found on a beach in Maine, a small bottle of lavender oil, and - don't laugh - a rubber duck. Yes, a rubber duck. It reminds me not to take myself too seriously, even in the midst of a flashback.

The key is to engage multiple senses. Something to touch, something to smell, something to look at, maybe something to taste - like those hard candies your grandmother always had in her purse. The science behind this is solid: engaging our senses helps pull us out of the trauma response and back into the present moment.

But here's the thing about self-care that nobody tells you: it's not just about bubble baths and scented candles. Sometimes self-care looks like setting boundaries that make you feel like a total asshole. Sometimes it's saying no to that friend who always drains your energy, even though you feel guilty about it. And sometimes - this is a big one - it's about letting yourself feel your feelings without trying to fix them.

Let me share a technique I call the ""Permission Slip Practice."" Remember those permission slips we needed for school field trips? Well, we're going to write ourselves permission slips for our feelings. ""I give myself permission to be angry about what happened."" ""I give myself permission to rest when I'm tired."" ""I give myself permission to not have it all figured out yet."" Write these down. Put them somewhere visible. Add to them as needed.

Another practical tool that's been a game-changer for many in our community is the ""Energy Account Balance."" Think of your energy like a bank account. Different activities either deposit or withdraw energy. The trick is to start noticing which is which for you personally. Maybe scrolling social media feels like it should be relaxing, but actually leaves you depleted. Maybe washing dishes while listening to a podcast actually helps you recharge. There's no universal right or wrong here - it's about getting to know your own energy economy.

Keep a simple note on your phone for a few days. Write plus signs next to activities that give you energy and minus signs next to ones that drain you. This isn't about judgment - some energy-draining activities are necessary parts of life. It's about awareness and balance. If you know you have a particularly draining activity coming up, you can plan to balance it with something energizing.

Remember, these tools aren't meant to be perfect solutions. They're starting points, experiments in getting to know yourself better. Some will work brilliantly for you, others might feel awkward or unhelpful. That's okay. That's actually valuable information about what your unique system needs.

And hey, if you try something and it doesn't work, that doesn't mean you're doing it wrong or that you're somehow broken. It just means that particular tool isn't the right fit for you right now. Think of it like trying on clothes - not everything in the store is meant to fit you perfectly, and that's totally fine.

As we wrap up this section on practical tools, I want to emphasize something crucial: start small. Really small. Like, microscopically small if you need to. One body check-in per day. One item in your emergency kit. One permission slip. Building a self-care practice is like building a house - you need a solid foundation before you can start working on the roof. And speaking of foundations, let's move on to some additional strategies that can help support everything we've talked about so far...

So here's something I've learned the hard way – and I bet many of you can relate – sometimes the most powerful self-care tools are the ones that seem ridiculously simple at first glance. Like, almost embarrassingly simple. Let me tell you about what I call the ""Five-Minute Reset."" When everything feels overwhelming, when your nervous system is screaming at you, sometimes all you need is five minutes of intentional presence.

Now, I know what some of you are thinking – ""Greg, I don't even have five minutes to spare."" Trust me, I get it. But here's the thing: this isn't about adding another task to your already overflowing plate. It's about creating tiny pockets of sanctuary in your day. You know those moments when you're hiding in the bathroom at work, or sitting in your car before going into the grocery store? Those are perfect opportunities for a reset.

Let me share something that happened just last week. I was having one of those days where everything felt like too much – you know the kind I mean. My trauma brain was serving up a greatest hits compilation of every mistake I'd ever made, and my body felt like it was made of concrete. Instead of pushing through like I used to, I stopped. Just... stopped. I put my hand on my heart, felt my breathing, and remembered what my therapist always says: ""You're having feelings, not solving problems right now.""

Speaking of feelings, let's talk about something that's been absolutely game-changing for many of our listeners – the concept of ""emotional weather forecasting."" This isn't about predicting the future; it's about learning to recognize your personal patterns and planning accordingly. Just like you wouldn't go out in a thunderstorm without an umbrella, you shouldn't face your known emotional triggers without proper preparation.

Here's what this might look like in practice: Maybe you know that Monday mornings are particularly rough for you because they remind you of stressful family situations. Instead of beating yourself up about it, you can plan ahead. Set your alarm fifteen minutes earlier. Have your comfort playlist ready. Put your coziest sweater where you can easily reach it. These aren't indulgences – they're strategic supports for your nervous system.

And you know what? Sometimes self-care looks like saying ""no"" to things that don't serve you. I had a client – let's call her Sarah – who always felt obligated to attend every family gathering, even though they left her emotionally depleted for days afterward. We worked together to develop what I call a ""declining with grace"" script. It's not about burning bridges; it's about building boundaries that protect your peace.

Now, let's talk about something that might sound counter-intuitive: scheduling worry time. Yeah, you heard that right. Instead of letting anxiety hijack your entire day, set aside specific times to process your concerns. It's like giving your worried mind office hours – ""I see you, anxiety, and we'll address these thoughts at 4 PM today."" This isn't about suppressing emotions; it's about containing them in a way that feels manageable.

One thing that's absolutely crucial – and I cannot stress this enough – is having what I call a ""self-care accountability buddy."" This isn't someone who's going to police your behavior or make you feel guilty. Instead, it's someone who gets it, someone who understands that recovery isn't linear, and someone who can gently remind you of your own strength when you temporarily forget.

You know what's funny? Sometimes the most profound self-care moments happen in the most mundane situations. Like doing the dishes – stay with me here. Instead of viewing it as just another chore, try turning it into a mindfulness practice. Feel the warm water, notice the soap bubbles, listen to the sounds. It's not about making the activity perfect; it's about finding presence in the ordinary.

And speaking of presence, let's talk about what I call the ""future self check-in."" When you're making decisions about self-care, ask yourself: ""What would my future self thank me for right now?"" Sometimes it's taking that nap. Sometimes it's making that therapy appointment you've been putting off. And sometimes – this is important – it's celebrating the small wins, like managing to brush your teeth on a really hard day.

Remember, creating a self-care plan isn't about achieving some perfect state of zen-like calm. It's about building a sustainable practice that can weather the storms of trauma recovery. Think of it like creating your own personal safety net, woven from all these different tools and strategies we've discussed. Some days you'll need certain strands more than others, and that's absolutely okay.

You know what really gets me excited about all of this? It's seeing how these self-care practices aren't just isolated activities - they're part of a larger story of reclaiming our lives. When we start putting these pieces together, something magical happens. The breathing exercises connect with the boundary setting, which flows into the mindful moments, and suddenly we're creating this beautiful tapestry of self-support.

Listen, I get that some days it feels impossible. There are mornings when just getting out of bed feels like climbing Mount Everest. That's exactly why we need this flexible, personalized approach. Your self-care plan isn't meant to be another source of shame or pressure - it's your lifeline, your permission slip to prioritize yourself.

The key is starting small and building gradually. Maybe today it's just five minutes of quiet breathing, or writing one kind thing about yourself. Tomorrow might look different, and that's perfectly okay. What matters is that you're showing up for yourself, creating that consistent message that you're worth taking care of.

Remember those listener stories we shared? Each one started with that first tiny step. That's all you need to do - just take that first step, however small it might seem. Your future self will thank you for starting this journey today.

Next week, we'll dive into dealing with triggers in daily life, which ties perfectly into everything we've covered today. Until then, keep being gentle with yourself - you're doing better than you think.

I'm Gregory, and you're tuning in to ""CPTSD: Let's Make Sense of This Shit."" Today, we're diving deep into something that affects pretty much all of us living with Complex PTSD – those sneaky, sometimes overwhelming triggers that pop up in our daily lives.

You know those moments, right? Maybe you're just going about your day, grabbing coffee, when someone's tone of voice suddenly sends you spiraling. Or you're in a meeting at work, and your boss's criticism – even if it's constructive – leaves you feeling like that scared kid all over again. These aren't just random reactions; they're our trauma responses showing up in real-time, and honestly? It's exhausting as hell.

In today's episode, we're going to unpack this whole trigger business in a way that actually makes sense. I'm not here to throw a bunch of clinical terms at you – though we'll get into some of that when it's helpful. Instead, we're going to look at how these triggers show up in our everyday lives, what they're trying to tell us, and most importantly, how we can start working with them instead of feeling ambushed by them.

We'll be hearing from some brave listeners who've shared their experiences – stories that I bet will sound familiar to many of you. And because I know we're all about practical solutions here, I've got some solid, real-world techniques that you can start using today to help manage these triggers when they pop up.

Look, I get it. This isn't easy stuff to talk about, let alone work through. But here's the thing – understanding our triggers isn't just about surviving them; it's about learning what they're teaching us about our healing journey. So grab your favorite comfort beverage, find a cozy spot, and let's figure this shit out together. Because while we might not have chosen our trauma, we absolutely can choose how we heal from it.

Before we dive deeper into today's topic, let's pause for a moment and do what we always do - check in with our bodies. Because here's the thing about triggers: they live in our nervous system, and getting grounded in our physical experience right now can help us engage with this heavy topic more safely.

So wherever you are - driving, doing dishes, hiding in your bathroom from your kids (hey, no judgment here, we've all been there) - let's take a few breaths together. If it feels safe, you can close your eyes. If that's not your jam today, just soften your gaze a bit.

Notice how your body is making contact with whatever's supporting you. Maybe it's a chair, the floor, your bed - just feel those points of contact. And yeah, I know some of you are thinking ""Greg, I'm literally scrubbing my bathtub right now,"" and that's perfectly fine too. Just notice where you are and how your body feels in this moment.

Take a slow breath in through your nose if you can, letting your belly expand like you're trying to fog up an imaginary window. Hold it for a moment - not straining, just a gentle pause. And then let it out through your mouth, like you're trying to make a candle flame dance without blowing it out. Do this a couple more times at your own pace.

Now, scan through your body, starting at your feet. Notice any tension, any holding patterns. Maybe your shoulders are trying to become earrings (mine totally were just now), or your jaw is doing that thing where it's trying to crack walnuts. No need to change anything - just notice what's happening in your body right now.

You might be aware of some activation as we talk about triggers - maybe a flutter in your stomach, a tightness in your chest, or that fun bowling ball of anxiety that likes to sit right in the solar plexus. That's all completely normal. Your body is doing exactly what it's supposed to do - it's communicating with you.

Remember, we're not trying to fix or change anything here. We're just creating a little space to notice what's actually happening in our bodies, rather than getting caught up in the stories our minds are telling us about what's happening.

Take one more deep breath with me. And as you exhale, just let your body settle into whatever state it's in right now. Perfect or messy, calm or activated - it's all welcome here.

Now that we've checked in with our bodies, let's explore how we can work with these triggers in a way that honors both our need for safety and our capacity for growth.

You know, I want to take a moment to really dive into something that I think gets overlooked when we talk about triggers - the absolutely wild intelligence of our nervous system. Like, seriously, the fact that we get triggered isn't a malfunction - it's actually our body's brilliant survival strategy working exactly as designed.

Here's what blows my mind: Every time we experience a trigger, our body is essentially saying, ""Hey friend, I remember something like this from before, and it wasn't safe then, so I'm going to protect you now."" That's fucking beautiful when you think about it. Misguided sometimes? Sure. But beautiful nonetheless.

Let me share something personal here. The other day, I was in a coffee shop, and someone dropped a metal tray. That sharp, clanging sound - boom - instant trigger. My heart started racing, my shoulders tensed up, and for a split second, I was right back in my childhood kitchen, where loud noises never meant anything good. But here's the thing - instead of beating myself up about it, I thought, ""Thanks, nervous system. You're trying to keep me safe. I see you.""

And that's what I want us to really get about triggers - they're not our enemy. They're more like an overzealous bodyguard who maybe needs some updated intel about what's actually dangerous in our current life versus what was dangerous in our past.

Now, this doesn't mean we have to like being triggered. Hell no. It sucks. It's exhausting. It can make us feel like we're constantly walking through a minefield. But when we start seeing our triggers as information rather than interruptions, something shifts. We move from ""What the fuck is wrong with me?"" to ""What is my body trying to tell me?""

Think about it like this: If you had a friend who kept warning you about dangers - some real, some not - would you just tell them to shut up? Or would you sit down with them, listen to their concerns, and gently help them understand which threats are current and which are echoes from the past?

The real mind-fuck of CPTSD triggers is that they collapse time. In an instant, we're both our adult selves and our wounded younger selves. The coffee shop becomes the kitchen, the friendly face becomes the threatening one, the present moment becomes every moment we've ever felt unsafe.

But here's where it gets interesting - and I mean interesting in that ""holy shit, our brains are amazing"" kind of way. Just as our nervous system learned to respond to these triggers, it can learn new patterns. Not overnight, not through forcing or shaming ourselves, but through patient, consistent reparative experiences.

And you know what? Sometimes understanding this doesn't make it one bit easier in the moment. Sometimes we're still going to get blindsided, still going to feel that rush of adrenaline, still going to need to step away and ground ourselves. And that's okay too. Because healing isn't about never getting triggered - it's about building a new relationship with our triggers, one that's based on understanding rather than fear.

Remember, your triggers are speaking a language - the language of survival. And while sometimes they might be speaking a bit louder than we'd like, or about threats that are no longer present, they're always trying to protect us. Our job isn't to silence them, but to learn their dialect, to update their information, and to gradually help them understand that we're safe now.

So let's put this into practice with something I call ""The Trigger Translation Exercise."" I know, I know – not the sexiest name, but hey, sometimes the most helpful stuff isn't wrapped in fancy packaging, right?

Here's what we're going to do together right now. Find a comfortable spot – and by comfortable, I mean wherever you are that feels safe enough to close your eyes for a few minutes. If you're driving or operating heavy machinery, just bookmark this for later because, you know, safety first and all that jazz.

Take a slow breath with me. We're going to think about a recent trigger that wasn't too overwhelming – maybe a 4 or 5 out of 10 on your personal ""oh shit"" scale. Got one in mind? Good. Now, instead of trying to push it away or analyze it to death, we're going to get curious about what this trigger is trying to tell us.

Imagine this trigger is like a scared kid trying to warn you about something in the only way they know how. Maybe they're pulling the fire alarm because they smell smoke, even if that smoke is just from your neighbor's BBQ. Their intention is solid, even if their method is a bit... dramatic.

As you sit with this trigger, ask yourself: ""What is this part of me trying to protect me from?"" Don't rush to answer. Let it percolate. Maybe it's saying, ""Don't trust them – remember what happened last time?"" or ""Quick, get out of here before they reject you!"" Whatever it is, just listen without judgment.

Now, here's where it gets interesting – and stick with me here because this is the good stuff. Imagine you're responding to that protective part with the wisdom you have now. You might say something like, ""Hey, I hear you. You're trying to keep me safe, and I appreciate that. But we're not in that old situation anymore. We've got new tools now.""

Keep breathing. Notice how your body feels as you have this internal dialogue. Are your shoulders softening a bit? Is your jaw unclenching? These are signs that your nervous system is starting to get the memo that you're working with it, not against it.

This whole process might feel a bit weird at first – like you're having a conversation with yourself in multiple voices. But trust me, it's not weird at all. It's actually a pretty profound way to start building that bridge between your triggered parts and your present-day wise mind.

Take one more deep breath and slowly open your eyes when you're ready. How was that? Different from just trying to power through or shut down the trigger, right? This is the kind of practice that gets easier and more natural with time. It's like learning a new language – at first, it feels clunky and awkward, but eventually, you start dreaming in it.

Remember, you can come back to this exercise anytime. The more we practice translating our triggers, the better we get at understanding their protective intentions and helping them update their information about our current reality.

You know what's also really powerful when we're working with triggers? Something I like to call the ""Time Travel Technique."" And before you roll your eyes thinking I'm about to get all woo-woo on you, let me explain what I mean.

So often when we're triggered, we're not actually responding to what's happening right now - we're responding to something that happened years or even decades ago. Our nervous system is basically running an old program, like trying to play a Nintendo game on a PS5. It just doesn't quite translate, right?

Here's what I want you to try next time you feel that familiar trigger response bubbling up. First, acknowledge where you are right now - literally, physically, in this moment. Maybe you're sitting at your desk, or standing in line at the grocery store, or dealing with your partner's raised voice during an argument. Whatever it is, plant yourself firmly in the present.

Then, gently ask yourself: ""How old does this feeling feel?"" Not how old are you, but how old is this reaction? Sometimes it might feel like you're five, or twelve, or sixteen. That's your trigger time stamp - the age when this protective response first got coded into your system.

Now here's where it gets interesting - and I know this might sound a bit strange at first, but stick with me. Once you identify that age, imagine your current self showing up next to that younger you. What would you tell them? What do they need to hear? What resources do you have now that you didn't have then?

I had a client - let's call her Sarah - who would freeze up completely during work presentations. When we did this exercise, she realized she felt exactly like she did at eight years old, being forced to read aloud in class while kids snickered at her stutter. Adult Sarah was able to tell eight-year-old Sarah, ""We got speech therapy. We're actually really good at public speaking now. And these people in the conference room are our colleagues, not bullies.""

The beauty of this practice is that it helps create a bridge between past and present. It's like your adult self can loan your triggered self all the tools, wisdom, and perspective you've gained since that original moment. And let me tell you, that's some powerful shit right there.

Remember, you're not trying to erase or override the trigger - you're helping it update its data, like installing a critical software update. Your protective responses aren't wrong; they're just working with outdated information.

You know what I love about this work? It's not about becoming some enlightened zen master who never gets triggered. It's about getting real with ourselves and building a life that actually works for us, messy parts and all.

Here's what I want you to take away from our conversation today: You're not broken. Your triggers aren't character flaws - they're survival skills that helped you get through some really tough shit. And now you're learning to update those skills for your current life. That's not just healing; that's evolution, my friend.

Start small. Maybe tomorrow morning, when you feel that familiar tension creeping in, just pause. Take that breath we practiced. Notice where you are right now, in this moment. That's it. That's your win for the day. Because each time you do that, you're literally rewiring those neural pathways we talked about.

And hey, you're going to mess up. I still mess up, and I've been at this for years. The other day, I completely lost my shit over a coffee maker that wouldn't work - full-on trauma response over cold coffee. But you know what? I caught myself, used the tools we discussed, and managed to laugh about it later. That's the real victory - not perfection, but progress.

Remember, you're not alone in this. Every time you practice these skills, you're joining thousands of others who are doing the same work, figuring out their shit one trigger at a time. We're all in this together, learning to dance with our triggers instead of being bulldozed by them.

So let's dive into what the research actually tells us about this inner child work, because I know some of you might be thinking, ""Greg, this sounds nice and all, but where's the science behind it?"" And honestly, that's a fair question – I geek out on the research too.

Here's what's fascinating: studies in neuroscience have actually validated what many of us have felt intuitively about inner child healing. A groundbreaking study published in the Journal of Behavioral Medicine showed that adults who practiced self-compassion – a key component of inner child work – demonstrated significantly lower levels of anxiety and depression compared to those who maintained self-critical patterns.

But it gets even more interesting. Dr. Bruce Perry's research on the neurosequential model of therapeutics has shown that when we're working with trauma, we need to meet our nervous system where it was when the trauma occurred. In other words, connecting with our inner child isn't just some fluffy concept – it's neurologically sound practice.

A 2019 study in the Journal of Clinical Psychology found that inner child work, when combined with traditional therapy, led to a 40% greater reduction in CPTSD symptoms compared to traditional therapy alone. It's like we're giving ourselves the secure base we might not have had as kids.

And here's something that might surprise you: research from the field of psychoneuroimmunology – yeah, try saying that three times fast – has shown that emotional neglect in childhood can actually affect our immune system functioning as adults. But here's the hopeful part: studies have shown that self-compassion practices can help regulate our immune response. It's like our bodies are literally healing from the inside out.

You want to know what's really wild? A longitudinal study tracking individuals over 15 years found that those who engaged in regular inner child work showed improved emotional regulation, better relationship satisfaction, and – get this – even better physical health outcomes. The mind-body connection is no joke, folks.

But let me break this down into something really practical. Dr. Kristin Neff's research has shown that self-compassion – which is essentially what we're doing when we're caring for our inner child – activates the mammalian caregiving system. This is the same system that gets triggered when we're taking care of a child or pet. It releases oxytocin and opiates in our brain, literally helping us feel safer and more secure.

And for those of you dealing with shame – which, let's be real, is pretty much all of us with CPTSD – the research is particularly encouraging. Studies have shown that shame resilience, which we build through inner child work, actually changes the way our brains process negative self-talk. It's like installing a new operating system that finally works for us instead of against us.

Listen, I know some of you might be thinking, ""But Greg, my trauma is too deep for this to work."" Here's the thing: a 2020 meta-analysis of trauma treatment approaches showed that even individuals with severe, complex trauma showed significant improvement when incorporating inner child work into their healing journey. The key finding was that it wasn't about the severity of the trauma – it was about consistency in practice.

And this part really gets me excited: recent neuroimaging studies have shown that when we practice self-compassion and inner child work, we actually see changes in the brain's default mode network – that's the part of our brain that's active when we're not focused on the outside world. It's like we're literally rewiring our internal dialogue.

The research also shows something really important about timing: healing happens in layers. A study in the Journal of Trauma & Dissociation found that successful inner child work often follows a pattern of two steps forward, one step back. Our brains have this amazing capacity for neuroplasticity – the ability to create new neural pathways – well into adulthood. And every time we show up for our inner child with compassion and understanding, we're literally creating new pathways in our brain.

Many of us who've experienced trauma, especially the complex kind that happens in childhood, develop what I call a ""protective perfectionist."" It's this part of us that basically says, ""If I just do everything exactly right, if I'm perfect enough, then I'll finally be safe and loved."" Let me break this down a bit. When you're working with your inner child, you might notice this intense resistance come up. That's actually your protective part trying to keep you safe. And instead of fighting it, we need to get curious about it.

Understanding this didn't immediately make the voice go away, but it changed my relationship with it completely.

Here's another mind-bender for you: Sometimes the resistance to self-compassion comes from a place of grief. Because when we start being kind to ourselves, we often begin to feel the full weight of how much kindness we didn't receive when we needed it most. And that shit hurts. Like, really hurts.

But – and this is crucial – feeling that grief is actually part of the healing process. It's like cleaning out an infected wound. Yeah, it's gonna sting like hell at first, but that's how we make room for the healing to happen.

Every part of you, even the ones that feel broken or scared or angry, played a role in getting you this far.

They've been carrying some heavy stuff for a long time, and they're probably pretty tired. Our job isn't to make them disappear; it's to show them that it's safe to rest now.

So as we move into talking about specific tools and techniques, I want you to keep these stories in mind. Remember that your journey doesn't have to look like anyone else's. Your healing path is as unique as your story, and that's exactly how it should be.

So let's dig into some practical tools you can start using today to connect with and heal your inner child. I want to share some approaches that have really worked for both my clients and myself - because let's be real, I'm on this journey right alongside you.

One of the most powerful tools I've found is what I call the ""Safe Space Dialogue."" Now, this might sound a bit woo-woo at first, but stick with me here. Find a quiet spot where you won't be interrupted - maybe your bedroom, a cozy corner, or even your car if that's where you feel most secure. Get comfortable, close your eyes, and imagine your younger self sitting across from you. What age are they? What are they wearing? Really let yourself see them.

Here's where it gets interesting - and sometimes a bit uncomfortable, I'm not gonna lie. Start talking to them, out loud if you can. Tell them what you wish someone had told you back then. ""You're safe now."" ""It wasn't your fault."" ""I see how hard you're trying."" Whatever feels Published and necessary. And then - this is crucial - let yourself feel whatever comes up. If you cry, that's okay. If you feel angry, that's okay too. All of your responses are valid here.

You know what's fascinating? Sometimes people tell me they feel silly doing this exercise, but then they experience this profound shift. One of my clients - let's call her Sarah - told me she felt ridiculous talking to an empty chair until suddenly she burst into tears because she could finally tell her eight-year-old self that she didn't deserve what happened. That's the kind of breakthrough we're looking for.

Another tool that's been absolutely game-changing is what I call ""Present-Moment Parenting."" When you're feeling triggered or overwhelmed, pause and ask yourself: ""What would little me need right now?"" Maybe it's comfort, maybe it's permission to rest, maybe it's someone to say ""hey, you don't have to be perfect."" Then - and this is the magical part - give that to yourself. Actually do it. If little you needs a hug, wrap your arms around yourself. If little you needs to hear it's okay to make mistakes, say it out loud.

Now, here's something that might surprise you - play is actually a powerful healing tool. Yeah, I'm serious! When was the last time you did something just for fun, with no productive purpose? Our inner children often got robbed of play time, either literally or because they had to grow up too fast. So try this: pick one activity that your younger self loved - maybe coloring, building with LEGOs, climbing trees - and give yourself permission to do it. No judgment, no productivity goals, just pure enjoyment.

Let me share a personal example. I kept a box of crayons hidden in my desk drawer for months before I actually let myself use them. I felt so much shame about wanting to color, like I was being childish or wasting time. But the first time I actually sat down and colored for thirty minutes, it was like this weight lifted off my shoulders. It wasn't about the coloring itself - it was about giving myself permission to just be, without having to achieve anything.

Another crucial tool is what I call ""Emotional Time Travel."" When you're feeling triggered, try to identify which age you're feeling. Are you responding as your adult self, or is this reaction coming from your twelve-year-old self? Once you identify the age, you can respond more appropriately. If it's your younger self feeling scared, you can use your adult presence to provide safety and reassurance.

Here's something really important though - and I cannot stress this enough - these tools aren't about fixing or changing your inner child. They're about building a relationship with that part of yourself. Just like you wouldn't expect a child to immediately trust a stranger, your inner child might need time to trust you. Be patient. Be consistent. Show up again and again.

One more tool before we wrap up this section - create a comfort box. Fill it with things that make your inner child feel safe and soothed. Maybe it's a soft blanket, a stuffed animal, favorite snacks, photos that make you smile, or a playlist of songs from when you were young. When you're having a rough day or feeling triggered, you've got this tangible source of comfort ready to go.

Remember, these tools aren't about doing everything perfectly. They're about creating a gentle, consistent practice of showing up for yourself. Start small. Maybe try one tool for just five minutes a day. Notice what resonates with you and what doesn't. There's no one-size-fits-all approach here - your healing journey is uniquely yours.

And hey, if you're feeling resistance to any of these tools, that's completely normal. Our inner critic can be pretty loud when we start this work. Just acknowledge the resistance with compassion and keep taking those small steps forward. Every time you show up for your inner child, you're rewiring those old patterns of abandonment and neglect, creating new pathways of self-trust and self-compassion.

You know, another really powerful tool I've found helpful - and I use this one a lot myself - is what I call the ""Time Travel Dialogue."" Here's how it works: When you're feeling triggered or caught in those old trauma responses, take a moment to actually visualize yourself at the age when that wound first happened. Maybe you're seeing yourself as that scared seven-year-old, or that lonely teenager. Then, from your adult self, have an actual conversation with that younger you.

What's fascinating about this practice is how it helps bridge that disconnect between our adult logical brain and our emotional child brain. Like, the other day, I was feeling that familiar overwhelm spiral starting - you know the one, where everything feels like too much? Instead of pushing through it like I used to, I paused and visualized little Greg, probably around age six, feeling totally overwhelmed by all the chaos at home. And fuck, it hit different, you know?

I could see how that little guy needed someone to just sit with him, to tell him it wasn't his job to fix everything, that he was allowed to just be a kid. And here's where it gets really interesting - when we do this kind of dialogue, we're actually rewiring those neural pathways. We're giving our inner child what they needed back then, but we're doing it now, in real-time.

Let me share another practical approach that's been a game-changer for many folks in our community. I call it the ""Safety Anchor"" technique. Think about it - when we're triggered, our nervous system is responding to danger that happened decades ago. So we need to actively remind our body that we're safe now. This might look like creating a physical space in your home that feels completely secure - maybe a cozy corner with soft blankets, favorite photos, or objects that ground you in the present.

The key here - and this is crucial - is to use this space before you're in full activation. It's like building muscle memory for safety. Spend time there when you're relatively calm, really letting your body absorb that feeling of security. Then when shit hits the fan, your nervous system already knows the way back to that regulated state.

Now, I know some of you might be thinking, ""Greg, this sounds great, but what about when we're at work or in public?"" That's where portable anchors come in. Maybe it's a smooth stone in your pocket that reminds you of your adult strength, or a specific playlist that helps you reconnect with your present-day self. One listener - let's call her Sarah - shared how she keeps a small photo of herself as a child in her wallet. When she feels those old shame spirals starting, she looks at that photo and reminds herself to be as kind to herself as she would be to that little girl.

Something else that's been incredibly powerful for many of us is what I call ""Permission Slips."" This isn't about asking anyone else for permission - it's about actively giving ourselves permission to feel, to need, to set boundaries. Write yourself actual permission slips: ""I give myself permission to rest when I'm tired,"" ""I give myself permission to say no,"" ""I give myself permission to take up space."" It might sound silly at first, but there's something really powerful about explicitly stating these permissions we never got as kids.

And let's talk about resistance for a minute, because it's going to show up - that's just part of the process. When you start doing this work, parts of you might freak the fuck out. That's normal. Those parts have been working overtime for years to keep you safe in the ways they knew how. So when resistance shows up, try thanking it. Seriously. ""Thank you for trying to protect me. I hear you, and I'm going to take care of us in a new way now.""

Remember, healing isn't linear. Some days you'll feel like you're crushing it, really showing up for your inner child, and other days you'll fall back into old patterns. That's not failure - that's being human. The goal isn't perfection; it's building a new relationship with yourself, one gentle moment at a time.

What matters most is consistency, not intensity. Small, daily acts of self-compassion add up to massive change over time. Maybe it's taking three deep breaths before responding to a trigger, or putting your hand on your heart when you're feeling scared. These might seem like tiny gestures, but they're actually profound acts of rewiring your relationship with yourself.

You know what really gets me excited? Seeing how all these pieces we've talked about today start fitting together like this beautiful puzzle. The inner child work, the self-compassion practices, those tiny moments of pause and presence - they're all part of this intricate dance of healing.

And here's what I want you to remember: this isn't about becoming some perfectly healed version of yourself. It's about creating a relationship with all your parts - even the messy ones, even the ones that feel broken or scared. Sometimes that means sitting with your four-year-old self who's throwing a tantrum. Other times it's about letting your teenage self know it's okay to feel angry as hell.

I get it - this work can feel overwhelming. There are days when I still struggle with my own inner critic, when self-compassion feels about as natural as tap-dancing in space. But that's exactly why we keep showing up, why we keep practicing these tools, why we keep coming back to this conversation.

Next week, we're diving into mindfulness and how it plays into our healing journey. But until then, remember: you're not alone in this. Your story matters. Your healing matters. And every small step you take toward treating yourself with kindness is a revolutionary act of healing.
