Next episode will be Rewriting Your Story: Moving Past Trauma	NEEDS REVIEW			"Hey there, fellow travelers on this healing journey. I'm Gregory, and you're listening to ""CPTSD: Let's Make Sense of This Shit."" Today, we're diving deep into something that's been a game-changer in my own recovery, and I bet many of you have been curious about it too – mindfulness and its role in our everyday healing from Complex PTSD.

You know those moments when everything feels overwhelming, when your trauma responses are firing on all cylinders, and you just wish you had a pause button? Well, that's exactly what we're exploring today. But don't worry – this isn't going to be some woo-woo discussion about floating on clouds or achieving perfect inner peace. We're talking real, practical mindfulness that works in the messy reality of living with CPTSD.

Throughout this episode, we'll unpack how mindfulness can be your ally in those tough moments – whether you're dealing with emotional flashbacks at work, navigating challenging relationships, or just trying to get through your daily routine without being hijacked by your trauma responses. I'll share some stories from our community, including one that honestly changed how I view mindfulness myself.

We'll also look at what the research says about mindfulness and trauma healing, but I promise to break it down in a way that actually makes sense and feels relevant to our lives. You'll learn some practical techniques that don't require you to sit cross-legged for hours or become a meditation guru – because let's face it, who has time for that?

What I'm most excited about is showing you how to weave mindfulness into your existing routine in ways that feel natural and sustainable. We're talking small, doable steps that can make a real difference in how you experience and manage your CPTSD symptoms.

So grab your favorite comfort beverage, find a cozy spot, and let's explore how mindfulness can become part of your healing toolkit. And remember, wherever you are in your healing journey right now, that's exactly where you need to be.

You know what? Before we dive deeper into all of this, let's take a moment together. I know we've been covering some heavy stuff, and I can feel my own body getting a bit tense just talking about it. So let's do what I like to call a ""quick reset"" - nothing fancy, just checking in with ourselves.

Take a breath with me, wherever you are right now. Maybe you're driving (keep those eyes on the road!), doing dishes, or curled up on that comfy chair you love. Whatever you're doing, just notice how your body feels in this moment. No judgment, no trying to change anything - we're just gathering information.

Notice where you're carrying tension. For me, it's usually in my shoulders - they creep up toward my ears like they're trying to become earrings. Maybe for you it's your jaw, your belly, or that spot between your shoulder blades. Just notice it. And here's the thing - we're not trying to force relaxation. Sometimes just acknowledging where we're holding stuff is enough.

Take another breath with me. Let it be messy and imperfect - because honestly, when is healing ever Instagram-worthy? Feel your feet connecting with the floor, or if you're lying down, notice where your body meets the surface supporting you. That's gravity doing its thing, holding you right here, right now.

You might notice thoughts popping up - maybe about what we discussed earlier, or your to-do list, or wondering if you remembered to feed the cat. That's totally normal. Our minds are like enthusiastic puppies, always wanting to show us something new. Just gently bring your attention back to your body, back to your breath.

One more breath together. And as you breathe, remember that this moment - this exact moment - is your body's chance to reset. It's like hitting the pause button on your Netflix binge, except we're pausing the chaos in our nervous system.

Feel that? That slight shift? Maybe it's subtle, maybe it's dramatic, or maybe you're not feeling much of anything right now. All of those responses are perfectly okay. This is your journey, and your body knows exactly what it needs.

Now, keeping that awareness with you, let's move forward together. Because everything we're about to explore becomes so much more powerful when we're present in our bodies, even if that presence feels uncomfortable at first.

You know what fascinates me about mindfulness in CPTSD healing? It's like we're all carrying these invisible backpacks filled with past experiences, and mindfulness gives us this gentle way to peek inside without getting overwhelmed by everything we find in there.

Let me share something that happened to me last week. I was doing dishes – yeah, the most mundane thing ever – when I noticed this tight sensation in my shoulders. Now, pre-healing Gregory would have ignored it completely or gone into full panic mode about what it ""meant."" But instead, I just... noticed it. Stayed with it. And you know what? That simple act of acknowledging without judgment – that's mindfulness in action, folks.

Here's where it gets really interesting. When we're dealing with CPTSD, our minds often feel like they're running this exhausting marathon between past trauma and future anxiety. But mindfulness? It's like hitting the pause button on that marathon. It brings us back to this moment, right here, where we're actually safe. And I cannot stress enough how revolutionary that simple shift can be.

Think about it – when was the last time you really tasted your morning coffee? Or felt the texture of your steering wheel during your commute? These tiny moments of presence are actually little acts of rebellion against our trauma responses. They're saying, ""Hey, I deserve to be here, now, in this moment.""

I know what some of you are thinking – ""Greg, that sounds great and all, but my mind is like a squirrel on energy drinks."" Trust me, I get it. My first attempts at mindfulness looked more like mental gymnastics than peaceful awareness. But here's the thing that changed everything for me: mindfulness isn't about emptying your mind or achieving some zen-master state. It's about building a different relationship with our thoughts and sensations.

Sometimes that means simply noticing that you're breathing. Sometimes it means acknowledging, ""Wow, I'm really in my head right now,"" and that acknowledgment itself is mindfulness. It's like building a muscle – you don't start by lifting the heaviest weights in the gym, right?

What makes mindfulness particularly powerful for us CPTSD survivors is that it creates this tiny space between trigger and response. It's like having a pause button where there used to be just an automatic reaction. And in that pause, that microscopic moment of awareness, we get to choose. We get to respond instead of react.

The beauty of this approach is that it meets you exactly where you are. Having a terrible day where even breathing feels hard? That awareness is mindfulness. Feeling disconnected from your body? Noticing that disconnection is mindfulness. There's no failing at this – there's just noticing and beginning again, as many times as we need to.

And let me tell you something that took me years to learn: this practice of gentle awareness, of coming back to the present moment over and over, it's not just a coping tool – it's actually rewiring our trauma-affected nervous systems. Each time we choose to notice without judgment, we're laying down new neural pathways. We're literally rebuilding our relationship with ourselves, one mindful moment at a time.

So maybe today, just for a moment, notice where you are right now. Feel the weight of your body wherever you're sitting or standing. Take in the sounds around you. This is mindfulness in action – simple, accessible, and profound in its ability to bring us home to ourselves.

So now that we've laid that groundwork, let's try something together. I promise this won't be one of those awkward meditation moments where I tell you to imagine yourself as a floating cloud or whatever. This is real, practical stuff that you can use anywhere, anytime.

I want you to stay right where you are – no need to get into some pretzel-like position or light incense. Just notice your breath for a moment. Not changing it, not trying to make it deeper or slower. Just noticing. Yeah, it might feel a little weird at first. That's totally normal. Hell, I still sometimes feel like I'm doing it wrong, and I've been at this for years.

Here's what we're going to do for the next few minutes. Every time you notice a thought – and trust me, they'll come barging in like uninvited relatives at Thanksgiving – just label it. Not judging it, just noting: ""Oh, that's thinking."" Could be planning, worrying, remembering, whatever. Just ""thinking.""

If you notice physical sensations – maybe your leg is falling asleep, or you're aware of tension somewhere – label that as ""feeling."" Sounds around you? ""Hearing."" Simple as that. Three labels: thinking, feeling, hearing. No need to get fancy with it.

You might notice your mind wandering off to that awkward thing you said three years ago, or your growing to-do list, or wondering if you remembered to lock the door. That's perfectly fine. Each time you catch yourself wandering, just gently come back to labeling. Think of it like training a puppy – you wouldn't yell at a puppy for getting distracted, right? Same gentle approach here.

Let's do this for about three minutes. I'll be quiet, but I'm right here with you. And if you're driving or doing something that needs your attention, just bookmark this for later.

How was that? If you found your mind ping-ponging all over the place, congratulations – you're human! That's exactly what minds do. The magic isn't in having an empty mind; it's in noticing when we've wandered off and coming back, over and over again.

This simple practice is like building a muscle. Each time you notice you've drifted and come back to labeling, that's one rep. You're strengthening your ability to be present, to step back from the chaos of trauma responses, even if just for a moment.

And here's the beautiful thing – you can do this anywhere. Waiting in line at the grocery store, stuck in traffic, lying awake at 3 AM. No special equipment needed, no subscription required. Just you, noticing what's happening right now, one label at a time.

Remember, this isn't about getting rid of thoughts or feelings. It's about creating a little bit of space around them, so they don't completely hijack our nervous system. And sometimes, that little bit of space is all we need to respond rather than react.

You know what else I've found really powerful? The ""Then and Now"" practice. I stumbled onto this one during a particularly rough patch when my own trauma responses were going haywire, and it's become one of my go-to tools for getting grounded.

Here's how it works – and I want you to try this with me if you're in a safe space right now. Think about something that regularly triggers your trauma response. Maybe it's a certain tone of voice, or being in a crowded space, or even something as simple as getting an unexpected text message. Got it? Okay, now here's where it gets interesting.

Take a moment to notice where you are right now. I mean really notice – the temperature of the air on your skin, the surface you're sitting or standing on, any sounds around you. This is your ""now."" Then, gently acknowledge that part of you that's carrying that old fear, that old pain. That's your ""then.""

The magic happens when we can hold both of these truths at once: ""Back then, I wasn't safe, AND right now, in this moment, I am."" It's like having one foot in each world, but consciously choosing which one gets more of our attention.

I remember working with this practice when I was dealing with my own fear of confrontation. My body would go into full panic mode anytime someone raised their voice, even slightly. Through this practice, I learned to say to myself, ""Yes, raised voices meant danger then. AND right now, I'm an adult in my own space, capable of setting boundaries.""

The 'AND' is crucial here – we're not dismissing or minimizing what happened. We're simply creating a bridge between our past experiences and our present reality. It's like updating our internal GPS, showing us where we actually are rather than where we used to be.

What I love about this practice is how it helps us develop what I call ""emotional bifocals"" – the ability to see both our past and present clearly, without one completely overshadowing the other. It's not about forcing yourself to ""get over it"" or ""move on."" It's about gently expanding your perspective to include your current resources and safety.

Try practicing this a few times a day, especially when you notice those old patterns kicking in. Remember, you're not trying to erase the past – you're simply reminding your nervous system that you're here, now, with more options and resources than you had before.

You know what I love about this mindfulness work? It's not about becoming some enlightened guru floating above all your problems. It's about getting real with yourself, right here in the messy middle of life. And sometimes that means acknowledging that this shit is hard, and that's okay.

I remember when I first started practicing mindfulness, I kept waiting for this magical moment when everything would click into place. Spoiler alert: that's not how it works. Instead, what I found – and what I see in my clients – is that healing happens in these tiny moments of awareness. It's in catching yourself before you spiral into that familiar anxiety loop. It's in noticing when you're holding your breath and consciously choosing to take that deep exhale.

Here's what I want you to take away from our conversation today: You don't have to do this perfectly. You don't even have to do it well. You just have to show up and be willing to notice what's happening in your body and mind with a little more curiosity and a little less judgment.

Start small. Maybe it's just taking three mindful breaths when you wake up. Maybe it's feeling your feet on the floor while you're washing dishes. Whatever it is, make it yours. Make it real. Make it something that actually fits into your life.

Remember, you're not broken, and you don't need fixing. You're a human being who's survived some really tough shit, and now you're learning new ways to be present with yourself. That's pretty fucking brave if you ask me.

Next week, we'll be diving into ""Rewriting Your Story: Moving Past Trauma,"" which builds beautifully on what we've covered today.
